RATING: 9.5
Compra obligada, no es un wargame, pero divierte como tal, mezcla la gestión de recursos con la lucha, dándole más importancia a los recursos y las construcciones de edificios, pero sin dejar atrás la lucha. Algo largo de jugar casi 3 horas fáciles si juegas con 5 jugadores. Poco azar. 
Divierte mucho, engancha más, eso si, terminas estresado de tanto pensar.
Para mi esta en mi top 3. Sabe mezclar luchas, autogestion de recursos, construcción, supremacias... reduce el azar, la torre le da un toque unico y compensado, ya que lo que el azar te quita la torre te lo devuelve. 
Puede tener un defecto y es el que quizas le falte algo más de luchas, aunque esto es por que para mi NO es un wargame. Y es bastante largo a 4 o 5 jugadores. Con lo que puedes terminar un poco saturado de tanto pensar estrategias. A pesar de la largura, se hecha en falta un año más en el juego, para cuadrar las estrategias.

RATING: 8.5
As expected, this game plays almost exactly the same as Wallenstein. In my opinion, there are two major differences: the bidding for starting position/player benefits and the layout of the board. Bidding for player position is a nice addition as it removes a random element from the game that I don't really feel adds to the game in Wallenstein. I like the board layout better in Wallenstein, though, since I feel that the elongated board of Shogun separates players later in the game somewhat.

RATING: 4
In Dirk Henn land, you don't play the game, THE GAME PLAYS YOU!

In this game you commit to taking ten actions, but don't really know what order you are going to take the last five of those actions. As a result you are playing more against the game, while trying to squeeze in some player interaction on the side.

The cube tower is a fun novelty and the only reason to play the game.

See also Wallenstein.

RATING: 7.5
Shogun is a very cool game.  The cube tower works out very well to resolve battles, and also looks and feels super when it's being used.

I like how Shogun gives you certain actions and allows you to assign a territory you own to take that action.  Each season (game turn), you plan by assigning your cards and then resolve them in order.  The first half of this order is revealed when the cards are laid down, but the second half is hidden until just before it's resolved.  This gives you enough information to plan smart, but not enough where you'll get bogged down by AP and second-guessing.

Everybody needs to build stuff in their territories to score points, but they also need to protect their stuff with armies and/or strategically attack their opponents to steal their stuff.

And then there's rice.  You need to feed the people in your territories, and if you don't have enough they will revolt and potentially kick you out of the territory (wiping out anything that was built there).  Each year, there are 4 event-style cards that modify gameplay somehow, and each has a negative rice amount on it.  You're shown these at the beginning of the year, but then they're shuffled and come out one by one each season, so you're not quite sure what the rice penalty will be when winter comes.  This provides variation and an interesting planning component each season.

I like playing Shogun because battles are fun, planning is interesting, and there are always power struggles over limited terrain and valuable buildings.  It's a big game that plays in around 2 hours.

RATING: 7.5
I like the fancy mechanics we've heard so much about - a truly impressive accomplishment. The combat tower works perfectly in speed and presentation. The rules are well worked and beautiful. However, it invites a terrible analysis paralysis problem - so be careful if you play with very thoughtful min/max type players. Perhaps a timer will help during the planning phase. Still, such a nice flow in this game. Once you 'get' the game system, it plays reasonably fast (although brace yourself for a few hours of play) and is highly engaging.

RATING: 8.5
I'm going to have to play this more often to get a really firm aspect on it.  However, it not too dissimilar to Wallenstein so I'll base these comments on that.

Strategy-wise, I prefer the turn bidding and special powers here to the sytem used in Wallenstein - it's something else to think about, which is good for most of us.  However, it does add to the game length, and the Wallenstein is pretty long anyway.  I don't prefer the Shogun event deck as although the one in Wallenstein was more random, it was also more edgy and variable.  I'm not sure where I am with the maps.  On the face of it, there are more corners to hide in (which is not a good thing) but there are also a few sneaky pathways via the sea-lanes (which is a good thing).  It seems to me that on the Sun map, it is advantageous to be in the corners - there's enough to attack from there and it's hard to be attacked.  On the Moon map, it's easier to attack anywhere and so I think the advantage of having more options from a central position means that's the place to be.

RATING: 8
Great pseudo-wargame/development game -- gotta love the cube tower and the theme is a major draw for a Japanophile like myself.  It would get a higher rating if both of the sessions I've played (each with five people) didn't start at 9 PM and end sometime after 1 AM!

Update:  Now that I've visited Japan, the thematic appeal of this game has risen even higher for me.  I also find that no matter how well I *think* I am working out my strategy on each successive play, two things happen. 1. I always, always lose, typically coming in last.  2. Despite the fact that I always lose, I keep pulling the box out of the closet and insisting we play it!  Being able to say "I build the shinto shrine in Nara we visited," "I'm building Himeji Castle," "Edo, been there, done that," add a little thrill for me, but mostly I think it's those colorful, colorful cubes shooting around in secret inside that damnable tower that keeps pulling me back!  And so, my rating of Shogun rises again.  Hinode!

RATING: 8
You are all battling to become shogun, by taking the most land building the best collection of theatres, castles etc. and by defeating your opponents in battle.

It is a stand out because of its combat system whereby small wooden cubes of yours and your opponents colours are dropped into a treacherous "cube tower" and the victor is the one who has the most cubes come out of the bottom. This adds a great sense of newness to this game and can cause some major upsets since any cube that does not come out of the tower will stay there for next time, and may be dislodged later to fall out and assist a players later battles instead.

It also has a nest planning mechanic where you assign each of the turn actions to one of your provinces, meaning you do this blind at the beginning of the turn, not knowing whether other players will block your moves, or maybe take that province, making your order in there null and void!

Can get nasty obviously, but a neat and exciting game.

Charitied off to smuffin

RATING: 7
A great game of applied strategy with interesting mechanics; true to the historical background and context. This forces you deal with food shortages and rebellious uprisings, whilst battling it out 'Risk' style for area domination. The game therefore responds to your actions and strategic decisions, good or bad. I feel more than one game is required to truely get to grips with the various concepts involved. The dice tower is great fun; adding even greater elements of calculated risk and just pure fun!
I didn't pick up on certain strategic elements until late in the game, but thoroughly enjoyed all the aforementioned elements.

RATING: 8.98
Since Shogun has area control and simultaneous action selection, it was kinda inevitable that I loved it, in spite of what is (for me) an uninspiring theme (note to self: gotta give Wallenstein a try!). An extraordinarily tense game! The replayability value is immense, even without expansions. There are three things that keep me from giving it an outright 9, however:

1) It's an AP lover's dream.
2) It's slightly, just slightly, too long for what Shogun delivers (and I'm a fan of long games).
3) Shogun is a fiddly game, and the (at times) unclear rulebook doesn't help matters.

I can barely see the similarities to Risk which are mentioned here.

[4-5]3-5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
An euro war game with some clever mechanics and relatively straightforward and interesting decisions.  

You have effectively 6 rounds of action in the game, and two scoring rounds.  In each of the 6 action rounds, your have only 10 possible actions you can take, and in each round, you assign one of the territories under your control to one of those 10 actions.  Turn order is bid upon with slight bonus capabilities given for particular turn order choices, and there is a random event that applies to all players that round.  In addition, the ordering of the ten actions in each round is randomized and the ordering of the first five are known, with the remaining five sequentially being revealed as you step through the first five actions.  Scoring is based upon building three types of buildings, and having building type majorities in regions on the board.  Battles are resolved through the use of the funny cube tower.

All in all a very reasonable design, but in the final analysis, it just came up a bit meh for me. I almost thing the game would benefit with some house rules about "taking over" other people's buildings rather than simply destroying them when you enter the territory.  As war games of this type go, I would tend to prefer Game of Thrones or Diplomacy, though both of those are way too long to play!

RATING: 7
PROS:
-) Complex decisions from a relatively simple ruleset
-) Always torn between different things to do
-) The cube tower!
-) Some luck is involved, but not game-breaking

CONS:
-) Hard to see all the information you need at a glance (the provinces lack amount of chests/rice produced on the map, the cubes will often obscure the number of cities), which leads to confusion and frustrating mistakes.
-) The action cards laying on the player mats will easily shift and cause more confusion
-) Some kingmaking issues
-) A grave mistake early on can cost you the game. This in itself is not "bad", but it means you have to play along for 2+ hours without a real chance of winning (which gives a REAL incentive to become an active kingmaker)

RATING: 3
I have played Shogun twice, and don't feel drawn to play it again.  I like the cube tower, the combat, the bits, etc.  What really hurts Shogun is the planning phase, which bogs down way too much.  There are a bunch of different decks to keep track of, and it seems like there's more time spent flipping cards to prepare the turn than playing the actual turn itself.  I was almost relieved when it came time to actually do something! 

Additionally, there's very little tension outside of using the cube tower, as only a few of the 10 actions taken every turn alter the board in a meaningful way.  If this game had a solid action order and got rid of about 3 decks of cards it would greatly improve.

RATING: 3
The longest 5 hours of my life.  This is a game that whether you stand a chance of winning is heavily set at the beginning of the game by a chance drop of soldiers through a tower.  The game does not start with everyone on an even footing and is based more on the luck of placing the right 10 moves in advance along with the luck of the tower rather than real strategy.  You have only six turns the whole game that have to be designated in advance with too many random variables which keep it from being anything close to controlable.  If you have a player who can't make up their mind on their 10 moves (20-25 minutes to lay down their cards) the game will feel like an eternity.  Better suited for hard core war gamers that want to get into the euro boardgaming scene.  Bring something to read between down times.

RATING: 8
A great hybrid war-game and euro (that tilts more heavily to the euro side).  The robo-rally style action planning stuff feels a little out of place here but it's still fun.

As is often said, the game is more about building than destroying.  Aggression has to be thoughtful and precise.  Simply taking territory for its own sake wont get you very far.

all that plus managing unrest amongst the peasants! 

On the downside the game is quite long.  Especially with new players.  The rule book says you are supposed to play 2 years (in game time of course...not actual time), but in the past we've called it after 1.

RATING: 9
A leírás alapján sejtettem, hogy egy kiugróan jó játékkal lesz dolgunk. A játék igazolta a sejtést. Nagyon tetszik a csatákat eldöntő torony mechanizmusa, aminek hatására az egyes csaták nem tekinthetők egymástól független eseménynek, még a birodalom legtávolabbi csücskében sem. A torony ugyanis olyan különleges módon alakítja a szerencsefaktort, hogy ha valaki veszteséget szenved el, később nagy valószínűséggel kompenzálva lesz. Kivéve, ha nem valamelyik ellenféllel, hanem a földművesekkel gyűlik meg a baja, de ez csak rajta múlik. A torony az oka, hogy nem lehet tutira menni, és ész nélkül nekimenni az ellenfélnek, még nagy fölény esetén sem: egyrészt beleszólhatnak a parasztok, másrészt számít az is, korábban hogy szerepelt az ellenfél a csatákban. Előfordult olyan (bár ezt valaki biztosan hibaként rója fel), hogy 1 fős sereggel elfoglaltam egy tartományt, amit 6 fős sereg védett. 
A játék újrajátszhatóságát növeli a 2 féle térkép, és több olyan kártyafajta is, ami folyamatosan változó sorrendjével kihatással van az akciók sorrendjére (nagyon oda kell figyelni, hogy mindig minden kártya hatását figyelembe vegyük). 
Hátránya a játéknak, hogy nagyon elhúzódhat, ha túl sokat töprengenek a játékosok az eseményeik kiosztásán. Szerintem túl sok a 2 év, 4 fő esetén 320 akciót kell végrehajtani (a lázadásokkal együtt akár 100-nál több csata is lehet egy játékban, minden ilyen alkalommal a toronyba kell dobni az érintett seregeket). Nekünk ez 5 órába telt, a szabálymagyarázaton kívül. Amit még furcsálltam, bár nem feltétlen hiba, inkább csak érthetetlen számomra, hogy bár a körönként 10 akcióból csak 3 kapcsolatos közvetlen építkezésekkel, 5 (7?!) pedig hadászati művelet, mégis a játék végén döntően az épületekből szerezhető győzelni pont. Nagy dícséret a játékra nézve, hogy hibáival együtt is újabb játékért kiáltunk! 10 pontot a fentiek miatt nem adok, de nincs messze tőle még így se.
---
A 2. játék során egy fontos dolog kiderült: a fejenként 62 bábú (ajánlom megszámolni megvásárlás után) fontos szerephez juthat a középjátékban, mint korlát. Kettőnket is érintett a 4 fős játékban, emiatt nem tudtunk felhelyezni újabb egységeket. Ilyenkor nincs más választás: HARC...
---
Újabb játékokon vagyunk túl, mint kiderült, az első két játékban hibásan télen is akcióztunk, pedig az csak amolyan lezáró kör. Így a 3 akciókörrel azért annyira nem jellemző, hogy elfogyjon a 62 katona. 5 parti alapján egyértelműen kijelenthető, hogy középen nagyon nehéz nyerni!
Ki akarjuk még próbálni a gyári alapfelállást is, és a másik táblát, mert eddig mindegyiket azonos térképen játszottuk.
---
8 játékon vagyok túl. A játék továbbra is tetszik, de rá kellett jönnöm, hogy nagyon durva a szerencsefaktor, és nem elsősorban a toronyban, hanem pl. döntetlen licitnél, vagy a téli lázadásnál a lázadó tartományok kiválasztásánál. Emellett ami még hibának tekinthető, az az, hogy a tartományok kezdeti kiválasztása 90%-ban el is dönti a végeredményt (értsd: aki középen van, veszít)...

RATING: 4
First Play: Jan-09-2008
I don't think I would play this game again.  The cube tower is very wonky.  It just doesn't make sense at all from a warfare standpoint.  With the cube tower, your odds of victory is not the same with the relative number of cubes you and your opponent throw in.  It is actually very counterintuitive.  You actually want to go to war in a different region first so that you can seed the cube tower, then go fight in the region that you actually care for.  The riots are counter-intuitive since they help you more often than hurt you, since you get to seed the cube tower for free.  Also, there is much more to be desired with the buildings.  The only difference between the buildings are points and the cost to build them.  Sure, the buildings get special abilities when they match the events, but you can't count on that.  90% of the time when you build those buildings, your motivation is only for points.  If you get extra perks, that' just icing on the cake.  So much detail, but in the end they do not really affect the game.  The events are very weak.  The bidding for turn order is not used at all.  The killing blow is that so little happens in the long game.  This game incorporates all the weaknesses of euros and multiplayer wargames.  Blech.

RATING: N/A
t
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow. Was a little hesitante when had a chance of playing this at a local gaming group. The cover was a little questionable and did not want to play another long Risk -type board game. BUT this was totally worth it! Although it was long game (3,5 h with explanations) it kept me intrested right to the end. There were so many strange but great mechanics. 
To start of have to mention the dice tower: NOT THE TYPICAL were you drop dice at the top and the tower "shakes" it and drops it down at the bottom. No. Here instead of dice, you take the attacking and defending unit CUBES and drop it down at the top of the tower, but ONLY SOME make it to the bottom and some get jammed. And this at the bottom count and the jammed ones are left to probably influence upcoming battles. Although it is similar to dice roll it still feels that you are more in control with the every cube counts aspect. And when attacking another player you always have the uncertainty of how many armies did they already have stuck in the tower. Sure you mathy guys get a blast of counting.
Second is how you give orders. There is a grid given to each player and cards reprsenting each area you have on map plus some empty ones. In the grid there are 8 different orders that you can give to your units (attack, increase army, build building, gather reasources etc) In the beginning of every round players fill the grid with their cards thus giving an order to a certain area. The mechanic challenges you, because you have to decide which place is safe enough for resources to use for that turn and which is not. The trick is that the same place cannot recive multiple orders. Great brain teaser.
BUT the real sugar that totally separates this from RISK the board game is the buildings. Although you do get some points at the end from area control the real key is the buildings. Without them you are screwed! There are 3 building types (palace, temple and theater) and these give you a point each from the areas that you control. EXCEPT you earn more points if you have a dominance of certain buiding in a province. 3 points from palace and 1 from temple. So although a key is to control land as much as you can, the real strategy is to civiliced at it! 
The game was a great experience and dont think that this is the last time i played it!

RATING: 8.5
This game needs an advisory not to play it with only three, as this closes out key shipping lanes that wrap the board around on itself. Without those pathways, it's far too easy to turtle.

With that being said, this game manages to be Wallenstein, only much, much prettier. I suppose that getting the latest edition of Wallenstein (which has the absurdly overpriced Tenno's Court expansion built in) would be better both in terms of cost effectiveness and in having a much more open map to fight in. I'm fine with owning Shogun, though.

Oh, the gameplay! In short, a bane to anyone with AP, but an excellent mix of long term planning and risk management, trying to determine which future actions will trigger and when and whether an opponent will attack before you get rice or gold from a crucial province. Everyone geeks out about the cube tower, but it's the planning, development, and above all the strict limits (both in terms of resources and actions) of how you can attack which makes this a nailbiter of a game.

RATING: 4.75
Shogun is a decent eurogame, that is hindered by its unsatisfying wargame core. With only 6 turns to do stuff and having each action during these turns available only a limited amount with money being tight and the possibility of key actions to be interrupted (if you allow it or your opponents play cleverly), the euro aspect of shogun is agricola-like hard and well done overall.

The wargame part of Shogun on the other hand is extremely unsatisfying. Attacks basically let you trade units mostly 1 to 1 with a arbitrary defender's advantage in provinces without unrest. The main goal of a war is to capture an opponent's building or interrupt and threaten his play. Due to the symmetry of wars and actions though, any effort to raid and overtake buildings can be neutralized just by recruiting as many units as you do (there's a maximum of 10 units recruited each turn). Since you can only recruit or move and the opponent usually has enough space to safely build his buildings at least 2 spaces away from a recruiting spot, so troop movements can be countered easily. Since there is no advantage from unit placement etc war resolution depends only on how many are thrown your way, which usually results in the best turtling and thus least attacked player to gain an overall advantage. Attacking is mostly punished and is only rewarded if your target is unable to properly defend because he is attacked on multiple ends (which is completely out of control and would be avoided by turtling).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Es un juego de 3 a 5 jugadores. Se desarrolla en dos años. Cada año tiene cuatro estaciones. Primavera, verano y otoño son turnos de juego e invierno es turno de puntuación (no se juega). En cada turno hay 10 acciones posibles que se suceden en orden aleatorio. Cada jugador debe asociar una acción a una sola de las provincias que domina (se puede farolear, hay cartas "para engañar"). La acción solo ocurrirá en esa provincia. La asociación que hace cada jugador es secreta. Después se va desvelando las acciones y se van aplicando en un orden de juego (que anteriormente ha sido objeto de subasta). Está muy entretenido. 
La forma de putuar es por número de provincias, número de edificios construidos y mayoría de cada tipo de edificio en una región.

Viene a durar dos horas o dos horas y media.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I can see the potential of this game to be a classic. More plays will confirm if this is the case. The central mechanic of choosing which actions will be taken for each of your regions makes for a very interesting set of decisions. One cannot be certain of the outcome of each season (round) since your opponent's play may alter the importance of your own actions during the round. Though this may seem chaotic, careful planning and predicting opponents actions is key to minimizing this. Perfect information of the first five of ten actions is presented, but not of your opponents intentions, as action selection is hidden at the beginning of the turn. Buildings are typically underrated for scoring, as too much battling takes away from building actions, and sometimes does not result in productive scoring, as provinces score only one point for ownership. Excellent design and balance makes for a tense game each turn. Combat resolution is swift with the unique dice tower that simulates the fog of war as army strength is not absolutely known by either side. Plays well with 4 or 5 players. This is the highest ranked game that I consistently win!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Quoted from forums:

"Shogun doesn't have any serious flaws in my view, and the expansion solves perhaps the only three of the minor ones: 1) bad luck in initial province draw; 2) turtling 3) uneven initial number of armies in the battle tower. All of them can be however quite easily fixed in a simpler way without buying Tenno's Court:
1) make everybody pick from three visible provinces, not two at the start of the game. It is enough to allow everybody to have a sensible starting position while still makes for a different game each time.
2) the above, together with players not letting each other to claim a region early on by placing themselves at opponents rear is quite enough. It won't make the game as dynamic as Samurai, but it excludes possibility of someone easily winning based solely on their initial position.
3) give each player one more gold chest for every army in the battle tower less than the player with most of them. It's not much, but if you fear it will change the game too much, you can allow for only one chest for every two armies fewer."

RATING: 7
A good, tense and very challenging area control game. Far superior to El Grande, IMO, with more decisions and a good deal more planning and risk management. The cube tower adds drama without adding the arbitrary swings of luck that dice can create, and the planning phase offers some nail-bitingly difficult turns. 

What I see as mostly positives, however, are also some of the game's flaws. The crucial importance of turn order, and also being able to control the order of only half of your actions, means many of your carefully thought-out plans will be ruined or wasted, so the game can be quite brutal. Still, it's a good game. Too bad there's no two-player option, as that would get this to the table more often. My husband is also not a fan, so this is likely headed towards the chopping block.

RATING: 7
Based upon only one play so far, I think Shogun is a very good programmed action game. There are some tough decisions to make about where to do everything, balancing going earlier in the turn order vs. taking a special bonus that would help you the most vs. holding the war chests for recruiting and building. Also, there is always some risk that you won't be able to do what you want in a given province if there's a farmer revolt or if someone attacks your province before its programmed action phase comes around. But I like the tension this adds to the game. Position is very important, and if your provinces are scattered, you'll have a much harder time supporting them and holding onto territory. On the other had, exploit and withdraw (letting someone else worry about rice provisions for the first winter), is a possible strategy for getting ahead in money, which can be great for making a big crusade for territory in the summer and fall of the second year. Battles themselves were less interesting (to me) than I initially expected, based upon all the hype surrounding the tower and its use in Wallenstein. But I do like that the tower it is an efficient battle mechanism that minimizes down time, turning what could have been an eight hour game into a two hour game. I'll gladly play again (and again), but two hours is a pretty big time commitment for a family with young kids, so it will be a while before it sees repeated play. I look forward to the time when I can.

RATING: 5
Shogun is definitely an improvement on Wallenstein, both from a game standpoint and graphically (although there are times when form has unfortunately trumped function). And the double-sided board is great - every game should do this.

Honestly, though I just don't get the appeal of this game, or why it's so highly rated on BGG (see my comments on Wallenstein for more). Of all the great and wonderful games out there today, this is ranked 13 by BGGers (as of today)? It's just glorified Risk, and honestly, Risk might be better. It's certainly quicker. I've played some Risk -- Star Wars: The Clone Wars recently, and mostly while I was playing Shogun I was just thinking how much more fun the Risk game would have been. At least the Shogun theme has more traction than the 30 Years War theme, which never made any sense.

Shogun is boring, methodical, process-heavy, and tedious. It has highly attritional combat that rewards waiting for your neighbors to fight so you can pick up the pieces. There is no drama or excitement to it. This is a game for fans of euros who really want to be playing Risk in their heart of hearts, but can't bring themselves to do it. But if you want that sort of game, there is far better stuff out there. I've mentioned the two Star Wars-themed Risk games, but I'd also take Nexus Ops or Friedrich. The only advantage of Wallensetin/Shogun is the lack of downtime. This is not trivial, to say the least, but you can't make a boring game good just by getting rid of downtime.

RATING: 7
I like this about the same as Wallenstein.  The bidding for turn order is a great addition to the system, and fixes a problem that I had with Wallenstein.  However, I'm not a big fan of the long, linear map layout; I much prefered Wallenstein's map where you had access to much of the map from wherever your current board position was.  In Shogun, it seems that you can largely ignore what is going on with the rest of the map, as it is often so far away from your position.  Overall, I think I'll make some modifications and just add the bidding system to Wallenstein to get the best of both worlds.

RATING: 7
My first "heavy" game purchase (though I'm sure there are heavier options out there!). I love it. Now, if I can just get my family members to play it more often...

The 10 choices each turn are wonderfully agonizing. I love the decisions, and the battle tower is great. This game might bruise some people with sensitive feelings, but I'll play this game anytime I can coerce others into it.

Update: finally got a chance to play this with 5 players, and it was wonderful. I'm still discovering subtleties about the game after 5 plays (I played this a handful of times before I started logging on BGG).

Update: another play of the full "two-year" game. I love this game. It is certainly ideal with five players. Because we keep teaching new players, I've not yet played this where we choose provinces at the start. Instead, we keep using the "standard" setup. I'd like to try this with the selection of provinces.

Update: four years... FOUR YEARS since I last played this game. And since then, I've picked up a number of "dude on a map" games, a genre I really enjoy. My enthusiasm might not be the same as when I first started gaming, but I think it's still a solid game. The angst in action selection per province will keep me coming back.

Update: Eventually sold this off. I like the game, but it is a notch below some more beloved "dudes on a map" games that I own.

RATING: 9
Each player assumes the role of a great Daimyo with all his troops. To do so he must deploy his armies with great skill. Each round, the players decide which of the actions are to be played out and in which of their provinces. If battle ensues between opposing armies, the unique Cubetower plays the leading role. Owning provinces, temples, theaters and castles means points when scores are tallied. Whichever Daimyo has the highest number of points after the second tally becomes SHOGUN!

RATING: 10
Alright, here's the deal.  SHOGUN is pretty much a more refined, slightly tweaked version of WALLENSTEIN.  With that in mind, this game under either title is far and away the best of the "heavy" Euros and it is also the best Euro outside of the SETTLERS games.  Much, of course, has been made of the combination of Euro-style mechanics and wargame elements and it's completely true- both are here, and the result is a compelling system that combines warfare, area control, economics, planning, and one of the most fun combat resolution systems since CRTs.  It's a tough game- every round is full of really complex, difficult decisions that can make or break you and the ever-present specter of peasant revolts (which seem to happen more than in WALLENSTEIN) is a constant threat.  As far as the differences, I'm not completely convinced that there's that much of a difference between these maps and the original- I wouldn't be surprised if an analysis showed that the number of territories and connections were very similar.  The bidding for turn order is OK but really sort of superfluous aside from the special ability cards.  The event cards are slightly different (better, I think) and the tower has a clear tray (very good idea).  I liked the Thirty Years War theme of the original better, but SHOGUN doesn't fail to provide a credible sense of warring in Feudal Japan and a much better sense of graphic design and layout goes a long way to giving the game a very slight edge over its predecessor.  As far as the length, I've seen people claiming that it's taken them 2.5-3 hours to get through half the game.  What the f$%k you guys?  This is a 2-2.5 hour long game.  Sure, there's lots of decisions but unless you're a microanalyzer or play with them then you can easily knock this out in the time specified, particularly if you do simultaneous building and revenue collection.  At the end of the day, SHOGUN (or WALLENSTEIN) is a better, more exciting game than PUERTO RICO, CAYLUS, or any of the other favored heavy Euros and it has a lot that will appeal to gamers like me who want something with a little teeth.  Completely essential for anyone looking for an accessible multiplayer wargame.  This was my 500th rating, and a great one to mark that milestone.

RATING: 6
Shogun looks like a wargame but really is not: you simply do not have enough actions to attempt serious conquest. Usually, you only want to attack for high income provinces or for majority in buildings. The game looks very good (rating up by 1). The dice tower is interesting but allows for some counter intuitive tactics (e.g. teleportation of units: add 10 cubes in one battle in one end of Japan and 5 will fall out in another battle in the other end of the country). Also, the tower combat is unpredictable. (rating down by 1) There are too many possible combinations with unknown variables (should I build a temple this turn, where? where the opponents are likely to build temples etc): this takes time and may lead to analysis-paralysis. (rating down by 1)

RATING: 5

Nov. 20, 2007: While initially enthusiastic about this game, a second play has shown the game to be somewhat dull and predictable. I could still be persuaded to play Shogun on occasion, but I'd rather not play it more than a couple of times a year. I'd also likely turn down any invitations to play the longer, four-year game. RATING: 9
Ahhh, sit down and play a friendly game of Shogun! You get to negotiate with your fellow players, plot against your fellow players and finally conquer provinces owned by your fellow players in a feudal Japan (that is if your armies find their way to the battlefield). 

The game art is great and I like how the board looks. The rules work and are fair. Then there is the battle tower...

...the cursed battle tower. You toss your armies into the tower when you fight with your mortal enemies and see what falls out. Some times you get the feeling that you are fighting the tower in addition to your enemies. It eats your armies and then when you are fighting in a corner of the map that no one (including you) cares about your armies pop out of the tower like drunken soldiers that rode into battle at Osaka but somehow ended up in a siege at Kyoto. Fabulously cruel.

Timing these battles well is key though, so it is not unimportant. I don't particularly like this game, but can't find much wrong with it either and can see how some would like it. It just feels a bit slow to me and lacks a spark of direction. The tower seems like a negative for me also as at least with dice I can calculate probabilities to some degree... the tower seems more random and chaotic even if it might not be in truth.

RATING: 7.5
The gimmick--the cube tower--is very cool.  Beyond the coolness, this one is more Euro than wargame.  Capturing and holding territory is not very important, so if you like empire building type of games you should pass on this one.  That said, there is plenty of surprises to give you headaches and keep you guessing, so Euro lovers may not like it so much either.  This could be a good compromise game for your AT and Euro friends, but then again, it may just piss everybody off.  I like it, and look forward to playing it some more.  I'll give it a provisional 7.5 after a half-dozen plays.

A few more plays, a couple of wins (finally), now I'm thinking 8.

RATING: 8
Wallenstein redux, with better production and art.  Only complaint is that rice and gold should have been on the maps.  The extra mapside is a real plus.   Very fun gameplay, with plenty of surprises that you don't see coming due to the way actions are planned.  And how about that tower?   One of the most unique components in all of gaming.   Combat is bloody and uncertain.   Civilian uprising and attrition are well-handled in a simple and functional ways as a result.   Points can be earned by conquest or development, and both methods (or a mix) are viable.   It's a unique and well-designed game, simple as that.

RATING: 6.75
A weird cross between euro and fighting: you get points by building buildings, and most points wins, but you also get points by holding territory, and you can punch the other players and take their stuff.  I like the programming aspect, but it can be pretty brutal.  If you tell a province to tax, and attack comes up before tax, another player can steal the spot before you get any money.  The game's only 6 turns long, so the loss of 1/6th of your income is a serious blow.  Not sure whether I prefer this version or Wallenstein, or which side of the map I like better.  Think they're all about the same.

_________
Clever orders selection and execution system and the cube tower is great. This game is perhaps a master piece in the balance of hidden vs open information. It gives you enough information to strategise but enough is hidden to drive suspense. Whilst the games subsystems are a phenomenal puzzle the core suffers from problems common to multiplayer war games. The best strategy is to try and duck out of conflict and just build through the mid game and swing hard at the end. Once everyone realises this the mid hour of the game can get rather dull. If you attack to early you just leave yourself exposed to the other players.

RATING: 8

RATING: 6
Fun, beautiful game.  Love the tower!  It's a planning game with an occasional battle, so the war element is secondary to the building.  The only negative for me is that Shogun has a lot of cards and small decks that you have to upkeep.  I wish it was more streamlined.  The game limits how many battles and actions you can take so it doesn't have a wide open feel like some other area majority games.  It keeps all the players in close step since they're all just arranging the same set of turn options.  Doesn't feel like much room for a sweeping stroke here- just be efficient and plan the best.  I'm not sure if that will get old for me or not... Logic lovers will adore it, but for me it has a feeling of Elfenland -planning ahead and plotting  your turn in advance.  

Note:  It's ironic that in a game of detailed planning the funnest part is the cube tower, which is so fun because it's so unpredictable!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Really starts getting tense in the second year - love this game in so many ways.  It took a few plays to understand how to effectively use battles.  I think a lot of people look at the map the first time and get into a risk/world domination frame of mind when really you can only hold on to a few more provinces than you start with and you don't get that many actions in the whole game to do much more.  It's more about morphing your territory to take advantage of the most building bonuses or making a grab of your opponents buildings.  Once you get that, I find the strategy very enjoyable.  Since there's a bit of chance, it's easy to find competition for this game, yet I constantly find myself in agonizing, brain-burning situations.  This one is definitely going to see a lot of play. 

Update: well, it doesn't see so much play :)  There's enough randomness that the hardcore players don't really suggest it that often, and it's long enough the lighter players don't suggest it either.  I still really enjoy this, but I've matured a lot as a gamer since I first discovered it, and there's just so many other games I'd rather play in that time frame.

I've played Wallenstein on SBW quite a few times and love it, and when I finally got the chance to play Shogun as a boardgame, I enjoyed it too - just not quite as much as I thought.  I love the decisions to make in it, and that most of the decision-making is done at the same time your opponents are doing theirs.  But it is a very fiddly game, and after being accustomed to the computer taking care of the details, it takes some getting used to.  Another minor gripe I wasn't expecting to have: the cube tower is very easy to bump if it's sitting on the same table as the board - just bumping the table can cause more of those hidden cubes to come down the tower.  I'm expecting my rating to go up to 9 after a couple of more plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Much the same as Wallenstein.  I just feel like I am bound by the rules, and that I never really get involved with what is occuring on the board.  It may sound odd, but I think Risk is a better engine than this.  The last comment is said largely because of expectation going into this game.  You want to feel as though you are at war, pushing boundaries and seizing control of an empire.  I don't get this feeling with Shogun. War is only part of the game in Shogun, and a very small part at that.  Shogun is more of an economic engine with resource management. The dice tower is a nice novelty, but it rewards in the same way as rolling dice does in Risk - Whomever has the higher number of troops has the better odds (this leads to another question of why people accept randomizers in some games and loathe others for having it).  I also feel there are a lot of touches in Shogun that are added on for gaminess, and are not really thematic in anyway - and maybe misguidedly I expected some theme from this title (and for me theme is never really a make or break).  Although I do enjoy simultaneous action selection games, the fact that a player can only do one action with a state seems strange (one of the gamey design decisions aforementioned).  I doubt that the entire military would be involved in collecting taxes, and therefore prevented from invading an enemy territory.  I find Shogun is at that middling (half-baked) stage where it suggests a lot of interesting ideas but falls short on execution.  Comparatively, as "hybrid euro war games" go this pales to a game like Friedrich, which offers the Euro mechanic with a real feel of warfare.    The game works, I however don't find it dynamic enough or living up to it's potential.  It's a game that I believe decieves euro gamers into thinking they are expanding their range, and has lived off of the hype of a hard to acquire sibling.  More work than fun.

RATING: 7
I'm still a bit disappointed that this wasn't a straight reprint of Wallenstein since I missed Wallenstein and am interested in the 30 Years War, which is a subject rarely covered in games.  Still, if they had to create a new game rather than a straight reprint, then this period of Japanese history is certainly one of the most interesting themes to use.

The game itself is very interesting both in the gameplay and in the operation of the cube tower.  The latter makes for a very interesting random element that introduces a level of uncertainty while at the same time almost guaranteeing that everyone will have a balance of good and bad outcomes over the course of the game.  

The result is a game that I hope to get more chances to play in the future.

Update:  Five years after writing the above comment, I still love this game, despite only getting in about 1 play a year.  Now that I have a copy of the reprint of Wallenstein, that may drop even further, but it's still a game that will stay on my shelf.

RATING: 6
This is the Euro version of Risk.  

How is it a Euro? Each territory you possess (up to ten territories) can be assigned to one of ten actions.  Two of the actions are attack actions, three are build point scoring monuments, three are to move/reinforce and one to get gold and one to feed your people.  Actions are performed by all players in a sequence that is randomly determine at the start of each year.  You win the game by having the most points at the end of the game just like most Euros.

How is it Risk?  Your starting territories are distributed randomly (OK each  time you are assigned a territory you can pick from two known or one totally random territory but it is still pretty random.)  Initially the reasons for attacking others are pretty vague other than "I had to attack someone to gain territory" or "You were next to me and weak" just like in Risk.  Battles are simply decided by the number of armies involved and by chance.  The cube tower is just a fancy version of rolling dice.

RATING: 9
[b] Shogun[/b] [i]Dirk Henn 2006[/i] 
A wonderful multiplayer experience. The game really shines with a full compliment of five players but it is very good with four. Use always the moon side of the board cause this side of the map is more open and corrects the ultradefensive tactics options that you can find in games played in the sun side. Shogun new rules about turn order are way better than old Wallenstein ones. The game is a blend between eurogame and wargame, although it is more the former than the latter since there are only six battles per year: twelve in total per player out of sixty actions. This is a fantastic game I wish I could have enjoyed with my teenage friends back in the eighties and nineties instead of Risk.

RATING: 6.5
One play with four players and one with three (and I've also played its predecessor Wallenstein once). Shogun is a game with a weird split personality; it has the rules and complexity of a heavy euro-type game, with a wonderful puzzle-like aspect to it at times, but then the players are left at the mercy of the cube tower, the order of the action cards, the special event cards, and the unpredictability of the heavy player interaction. Individually those four things aren't overly chaotic, but when you add them all together you create a huge amount of unpredictability. Anyway, it seems to want to be both a thinking-man's game and a free-for-all, and it ends up doing neither idea justice. 

Overall I wouldn't say that I dislike it, and I think there's definitely some potential for fun in there, but I also feel like there's potential for frustration, and that keeps me from getting terribly enthusiastic about it.

RATING: 6
It's a little too static, a little too dependent on initial setup, a little too chaotic, and a lot too long. But man, that tower is cool, fun, and a great mechanic as well. I suspect that a better, shorter game is lurking underneath.
[EDIT]
I have now finally settled my opinion of the Wallenstein-Shogun-Wallenstein game "engine", as it were.
-First, everyone at the table should internalize what it isn't--it ain't a battle game. It's a building game where your products and infrastructure are vulnerable. If you think of it as a battle game, the limited opportunity for fights and the relatively static map will irk you.
-Second, initial territory selection is key. Like Settlers, you can lose the game before it begins in earnest if your initial selection is poor. Fortunately, there is a recommended initial setup for new players.
-Third, the Court expansion is quite good. It adds very little to the playing time (maybe 5 minutes?) and helps mitigate the static map by making useless armies usable. 
-Fourth, it is best when played briskly. Very briskly. More briskly than people other than me seem capable of. When you've got possibly 5 at a table, the odds that someone will (almost single-handedly) drag what ought to be 90 minutes to 180 is high. This is sad. At a reliable 90 minutes, this is a very good game. I have yet to play that "version", however. 
The duration of the game is the real killer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It's one of my favourite games. 

Based on the classic Wallenstein which I really must play.

I like the theme.

The board and the components are attractive.

You have difficult choices to make which can be agonising - but that's half the fun. It helps if you can correctly anticipate what the other players will do. That inevitably involves some luck. Getting that judgment wrong can cost you dear - in one game I played one player tried to build rice in a province, unfortunately that region was taken off him before he could get his rice, it was a big set back.

In games where players take turns to move everyone has to wait passively while each player in turn thinks about and then conducts their move. This means that in heavily analytical games you can, unless helped along with the aid of an egg timer, suffer the dreaded "analysis paralysis". You don't get that in Shogun. Players determine their actions simultaneously. So everyone is actively thinking about their move at the same time. It is a brain-burning game and it is nice to deliberate over you moves. If everyone takes a similar amount of time selecting their moves people won't have to endure the long waiting times that you get with some games.

The battle tower combat system is fun and original. Combat is a valuable tool used judiciously. It is bloody - too much combat will drain you - you won't win the game.

In a lot of empire building games being in the centre is generally bad. In Shogun a central position leaves you vulnerable to attack. However, you have more options and access to more regions. Concentration in one region will limit you. In the games I have played people in a central position have often done well.

RATING: 5
This game disappointed.  Overrated and overly abstract.  The dice tower is cool from an abstract perspective but totally craps all over the theme.  If enough cubes get stuck and fall out at the right time they can basically teleport all over the board.  Very odd mechanic for battles.  I do not mind the luck element in games and I do not mind long games, but this game has a bad combination of the two and has very few decisions for a game of its length.  The decisions are gut wrenching but its not because they are particularly interesting but because the game is so luck driven you feel like when you get a chance to effect the outcome you have to make the most of it. There are so few turns in the game that you don't feel like you can really get your strategy rolling before the end.  Too long for what it is.  

Looking back, I think I was expecting more of a wargame and instead I got a shitty, overdone version of el grande with most of the good parts removed.

This game also has one of my pet peeves:  it looks wargamish, but the winner will nearly always be the player who was in the fewest number of combats.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[Initial Impression: 6]
I find it interesting that you're often weakening your position by attacking, and that because of the limits on actions there is a constant tension of wanting to do more than you're able. (You'll get at most 12 attacks for the entire game, and you can only use a given province to either harvest, fortify, or attack on a turn, so it is difficult and expensive to build up an army and then use it to attack over and over again - and you'd do so at the expense of leaving other provinces weakly defended.) 
The cube tower provides a neat uncertainty to battle resolution - I think of the cubes hidden in the tower as your allied ninjas waiting to ambush your opponents if they have done a poor job of tracking how many cubes you likely have in the tower. Because of these hidden cubes, it is even possible to take over a province and have more armies there than you actually sent into the battle.The main problem I had with the game is that too much of the decision-weight is front-loaded and made with less than half of the necessary information, so it involves a lot of guesswork regarding the likely actions of other players (which you can predict and respond to, so this is not necessarily bad in my opinion) and the likely sequence of actions later in the turn (totally unpredictable, yet sometimes damning). You then carry out these programmed actions with exactly 3 (relatively minor) decision points. The game feels like it lurches along, with everyone thinking through their programming, followed by a fairly scripted performance of the inordinate amount of housekeeping required to carry out the actions. There is also a possibility of the initial placement (which has a large random element) being disproportionately beneficial or harmful to some players. Without a concerted effort (read: near-collusion) by the disadvantaged players throughout the game, those players so advantaged are highly likely to win. I can see why some people like it, and it is an interesting take on an all-or-none "wargame", but I personally don't get the enormous appeal it seems to hold for some.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Dirk Henn classic. From the creator of one of my all-time favorite games, Alhambra. This game have already been published once with the different name and the theme. With this version, which for me thematically works a bit better, theme is changed from Center-Europe to Japan, Feudal-Japan, to be more precise. My personal favorite era of Japan. The actual game hasn't changed much from the different themed predecessor. It's still about area control, it's a kind of mix between Euro-game and a war game. How that works for people, it's something that everyone must decide by themselves. The game has this not too often seen cubetower mechanic. There's a cart board tower in which players during gameplay throw a varying number of different colored cubes (with different functions, obviously). Then when it is time to do actions involving the placement of the cubes, it's solely about the tower and a luck element it represents that what kind of cubes are coming out. I personally like this mechanic (some other games just use opaque container or bag to do the same thing).
But even though I like the designer, I like the game on premise level, I haven't found time to play and thus rate the game yet. Too bad it requires 3 players at minimum, because I am often playing my games with two players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Economics, territory management and war, just in the right ammounts to make the game flow extremely smooth and enjoyable for every one. This is a game that never fails to bring a memorable session. The more the players, the better the experience. Almost 15 years after the first edition (called Wallenstein) no other conquest game has beated this masterpiece.

RATING: 8
First play - still trying to figure it out. Combat and revolts are not explained clearly. It sets up like Risk, but with very limited actions, does not flow like Risk. With only 8 turns/seasons, and of those, 6 are seasons where actions can be taken. Each season, there are only 2 combat actions out of the possible library of 10 actions, so there are only 12 opportunities to attack the entire game. You can only gather rice 6x, gather taxes 6x, build castles/temples/theaters 6x. Strategy is how to maximize return on so few actions. Rating '6', due to initial poor player experience.

Update (07/07/11): It has been nearly three years since the first play. With 5 players, this was a blast! I finally "get it" why this game is ranked so high on BGG. Combat is deadly and unpredictable, with the cube tower simulating the uncertainties of war in feudal Japan: pissed off peasants, wandering ronin, surprise defections, enlistment in the field, logistics, etc., all in one massive cube throw--simply brilliant. Now having played Ad Astra, with its programmed sequence of actions, it is easier to approach Shogun's action programming mechanic. However, there is yet another element of uncertainty where the game dictates when your actions are triggered; you control what province it is triggered it. One must approach the game not to conquer, but to consolidate, as there are so few actions. Like EVE: Conquests, the game progresses ever so slowly, so reacting is not an option; one must plan ahead several actions and several seasons.

The rules are still shaky, but the production is top-notch. The board is striking, and the province cards are highly functional; locating them is very easy due to color coding and relative position right on the card. (The business cards in Rails of New England could use this kind of treatment.)

The decisions are numerous, and the desired outcome is unlikely, but if you can predict and string together a sequence of actions to net successful outcome, there is no kind of gamer high like this one! I am upping this to a solid '8', hoping to get this to the table more!

Update (06/09/12) 3 plays: Finally got it out to the table after nearly a year and it was a blast with two other Wallenstein veterans. Almost forgot the bidding for turn order and associated bonus card (for +1 coin, +1 rice, +1 reinforcement, +1 defense, +1 attack), which could steer the game in another direction. The random event cards mix it up even further. However, in a 3-player game, the narrowness of the board can make for a lopsided game. The two players who manage to get a good foothold on either end of Japan will do well in consolidating, while the third player (that's me) is stuck in the middle and in a tough spot as he gets whittled away on both sides. The current rules allow for drafting of provinces, and then it becomes a game of chicken: who will be "policeman of the world" and try to prevent one player from getting an area majority on either end? Unless all players keep each other in check, equally, there is going to be a breakdown in how the provinces divvy up.

If playing 3-player, I would suggest randomly distributing province cards (similar to the "French Rules" in Risk), and giving the players an additional challenge in trying to consolidate their fractionated empire. Otherwise, it seemed easily predictable who was going to win, based solely off the province draft.

Update (06/16/12): Played 3-player and 4-player. 4-player is beginning to look like the sweet spot. After playing a 3-player again with the original drafting rules, I am beginning to drift away from the random distribution. You probably need some group cohesion for the game to work. With only 12 combat actions and six moves, it may be tough. Still, we should probably take a look at the totally random distribution start and see if the feel is different.

Update (06/22/12): Played 3-player several more times and coming to conclusion that original drafting rules are fine. Drafting or not drafting specific provinces is part of the overall strategy of the game. Some drafts are going to suck, while others will seem ordained; that's the nature of the game. It ends up with what you do with what you are dealt, and it also depends on the temperament of your neighbors.

To conclude, I don't understand its hype. Or my brain is just not large enough to grasp its complexity.

RATING: 9
Man, the Battle Tower is fun!

Compared to most people who like the same kinds of games that I do, I am probably more tolerant of luck in a game. The Battle Tower, however, isn't lucky. In any individual battle somebody might get lucky and have more of their cubes fall out, but that just means you have more of yours left in the Battle Tower. Over the long run, the player who puts more cubes into the Battle Tower will win more battles.

This game is a crazy mishmash of mechanics that somehow work well together. There is a mix of open/hidden knowledge, vaguely Risk-esque attacking mechanisms, victory point buying in an area-majority sort of scoring, and only in six turns! The game can get a bit long and AP prone, but it is a great combination of traditional Euro and attacking elements. And a Battle Tower!

RATING: 7
Really the same game as Wallenstein so it garnishes the same rating and comments from me (see below). There is one big improvement in that Turn order is now decided by an auction rather than randomly but that is no where near enough to save the game for me. I don't remember if Wallenstein had random set-up or not but I certainly didn't like it here. I'm not sure about this map either, it looks like the North-East corner is a strong vantage point and I think the Wallenstein one was better in general.

================================

A very fun game if you can stand the randomness and unpredictability, this game has it in spades, maybe more than any other I know which unfortunately leads to some frustration. 

Still a good amount of strategy and tactics to be had here (just don't count on your plans working out), choosing your actions is really prone to AP, there is a ton to think about, especially as things progress and you are required to 'outguess' or try to anticipate your opponents moves as well. The tower is quite cool and innovative for solving conflicts and not near as random as some other things in the game, the main one being the order of the actions (the first 5 randomly set out and the last 5 random and blind!), this order of actions can really "make or break" your plans and the plans of others and gives you very limited control over what you and others are doing. 

Conflicts are very hard to carry out as the game is so restrictive in your actions, one province can only do one thing per turn and this makes it much more difficult and requires serious planning ahead and even then it may not be desirable anymore by the time you are ready for it. Good game despite these things but it does run somewhat long, especially with 5 players. Reminds me more of a restricted and random Risk with some nice added elements than anything else, only a little like Grande in the choosing of actions and scoring but this isn't so much area influence as it is resource management and war.

Update: 8 => 7 A second playing of this through the new 'Shogun' version (very little difference in core system) drops the rating and even the wife didn't like it this time and she loved it the first time (after she has played many more games she now realizes the negative effects of randomness). By the BGG rating system this would rate somewhere between a 2 and a 3 because I really don't ever want to play again, I think I would rather play Risk (and that is not saying much) as even it has more control, dice or no dice. It is a well designed game albeit it is full of randomness and therefore fine for some people just not for me. Everything about the game is randomly determined; Turn order, event cards, action sequence & combat resolution with the tower. One hour or less games with random/luck elements can be tolerated but this is a 3 hour game. My rating is for what I think this deserves as a 'game' and not for my desire to play. I might be a little too generous here though and I do think this is over-rated on the Geek IMHO.

RATING: 8
Blend of Euro and strategy game. 

I like it a lot. The pre-plotted moves require some thought and probability/risk decision making based on partial knowledge of the turn sequence. Will blue attack me? Should I therefore spend money to reinforce, or take a risk and use the money to build a points-scoring castle instead? Should I gamble on building a temple, hoping the income phase is drawn before the temple build phase so I'll be able to afford it? And so on...

The good news is that as everyone plans simultaneously there's little downtime, so you're not staring at the ceiling for half the game waiting for your turn to come around as you are in many multiplayer strategy games.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The game is a fight for cultural dominance. This is achieved through claiming and defending territories and building castles, temples and no theaters. The game takes place over 8 turns, but two of those turns are scoring turns, so players only have 6 turns in which to perform actions. Each turn consists of the same 10 actions that everyone takes, but the order of the actions is randomly determined each turn. Also - and this is the heart of the game - each action can only take place in a single province. Provinces and actions are married together at the beginning of the turn. Since you don't know how the other players are allocating their actions and provinces and you don't have complete information as to what order the actions will unfold, there is a lot of risk that needs to be taken into account when you make your choices. The actions include building one of the 3 types of buildings, recruiting small, medium or large armies to a province, 2 move/attacks, confiscating rice (you need to be able to feed your provinces at scoring or risk revolt), and collecting taxes. Also, players put in a bid for turn order which also grants a special ability for the current turn, depending on what is selected.

It is easy to get caught up in building armies and attacking and then neglecting the building side of things. Buildings are where most of the points lie, so you can't ignore development. Provinces are connected to larger regions and players with the most buildings of a certain type in a region scores bonuses. Combat is performed through a kitchy little device called the cube tower. Basically both the attacking and defending armies (wooden cubes) are dropped into a tower that contains lots of little ledges inside of it and whomever emerges from the tower with the most cubes is the winner. It is a quick and efficient way to resolve combat and in our game the results seemed reasonable given the odds of the battles that were fought. Occasionally, you might take a province and end up with more armies there than you invaded with (as some additional armies of yours get dislodged from the tower). It's almost as if your opponents dropped their arms and joined you!

The game is definitely very good. There is an interesting balance of chance and strategy that make the decisions difficult. I could see how the amount of chance in the game may bother some people, especially given the game's length (3+ hours).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Much simpler than I expected. 

Shogun is the poster-child for procedural gaming. There are six turns, and at the beginning of each one players all program 10 actions in secret. The catch is that they only know the resolution order for the first five actions they program. Available actions include two attacks, three builds (theaters, palaces, temples), two raise army actions, a reinforce, and two taxations (for rice and gold). 

It sure seems to advertise itself as an action-packed samurai wargame, but in fact it's a very staid game of building and territory management. Each player can only attack 12 times during the whole 2-3 hour game. It's not about taking loads of territory and wreaking havoc, but instead about judicious use of provinces and placement of buildings. The whole game resides in the overlong action-programming phase. It's tiresome. The cube-tower feels like a gimmick to me. 

Shogun is a thoroughly mediocre game. The most serious flaw here is the map. One side is terrible. One side is merely bad. On either side, simply staking out territory on either edge (long, horizontal island) makes protecting your score a breeze compared to players who started in the middle. I have not played Wallenstein, but the smart money says it's far superior to this supposed "development." 

Update: Having played Wallenstein, I can confirm that Shogun is a step in the wrong direction.

RATING: 10
This is a fantastic game w/ excellent, creative mechanics.  The mechanic of determining the outcome of combat is highly innovative where players drop their army cubes down a tower chute & whoever has the most troops drop out onto the collection tray, wins.  It is a strategic, diceless game which is very easy to learn & is a great gateway to players who are not into territory aquiring games & more into resource/objective-style board games.  I can see why this is a highly addictive game.  After one session of SHOGUN, I am ready to play it again.  It is a game which is worth EVERY penny you spend on it.

RATING: 8
Based on 4 plays.  Shogun is a tense game of economics, logistics, and warfare.  The amount of combat in the game seems just right - you can never feel too cozy on your borders, but it never devolves into Risk-like massacres.  Rather, military might tends to be a tool used for specific purposes, a feature rarely seen in games.  And the cube tower is just great...

That being said, the game does last a while, and with a slow player the initial programming stage can drag.  I also recommend tweaking the rules a bit (keep the pre-game seed cubes in the tray, reveal the active event before players program their actions) to ameliorate needless luck.  But these criticisms do not mitigate how fun the game itself is.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
At its heart, this is an area-control hybrid game that looks like a Risk-esque wargame.  I love that there are so many difficult choices to make each round, being forced by limited resources, a limited number of actions, and lots of imperfect information.  There's still a lot I need to learn about the best strategy to use, and I've been surprised how easy it is to get such a heavy and relatively long game to the table in my gaming group.  Its theme, beautiful board and bits, and especially the ultra-cool cube tower just attracts people to it.

Quality rating: 7
Willingness to play rating: 6

Great theme and map, but I needed more out of it, maybe the moon side of the game would fulfill that desire.

W/F average: 6.5
Final average: 6.75

RATING: 8
Only played Summer board so far. Nice area control / action selection / resource management game with a unique combat resolution system. Can’t underestimate the Winter phase as the loss of certain areas due to the inability to feed can have a big impact on score. Also, many aspects of the game are set up to dissuade conflict so battles should be initiated only upon limited circumstances. With turn order tied to a random bonus card, it often creates additional decisions having significant impacts. Looks to play best with a 4 or 5 players.  Predetermined setup is helpful for a balanced start.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Japan during the Sengoku or “Warring States” Period: each player assumes the role of a great Daimyo with all his troops. Each Daimyo has the same 10 possible actions to develop his kingdom and secure points. To do so he must deploy his armies with great skill. Each round, the players decide which of the actions are to be played out and in which of their provinces. If battle ensues between opposing armies, the unique Cubetower plays the leading role. The troops from both sides are thrown in together and the cubes that fall out at the bottom show who has won immediately. Owning provinces, temples, theaters, and castles means points when scores are tallied. Whichever Daimyo has the highest number of points after the second tally becomes – SHOGUN!

Shogun is based on the Wallenstein (first edition) game system.

RATING: 4.5
[2007.Mar.07] This re-themed version of Dirk Henn's classic Wallenstein by Queen Games is an attractive production with a great looking game board, lots of wood, and game cards with appealing art. [My only criticism is that the card stock used for the cards themselves is a little thin; you cannot riffle shuffle these cards.] The cube tower combat resolution system is unique and quite innovative. I like the extra effort by Queen Games to provide a two-sided game board, thereby allowing for hours of additional (and different) play. Nice touch!

[2007.Sep.12] The downside is that there are numerous sources of chaos and randomization which prevents any real planning during the game.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I would like to love that game but there is some things that are annoying. Maybe they could be fixed with some variants (^_^).

First there is way to much information to analyze and consider in your decisions. Placing the provinces cards on the actions is enough, having to consider season effects, special ability cards, the order of action resolution just makes it too much.

Second, the fact that everybody does generally almost the same amount of actions every turn of each type makes a game where everybody develop equally and gives up a game where there is few changes going every turn. It also makes a game where you cannot focus on certain aspects more than others.

Thirds, I just find it stupid that you can only do 1 action of each type. First because collecting grain from 1 province each month is almost impossible to exceed the food requirements. If it's hard to meet the food requirement it also mean that it would be impossible to take over all japan because the game prevent you from doing do. But it does not make sense, more province = more food = more things to maintain. 

Also the other things that annoys me is that since you only have 10 actions possible, having more than 10 territories is useless because you will end up up with unused territories. So to prevent both these problems it would have been interesting to play more than 1 action of each action type.

The combat tower is a good and a bad idea. The mechanic is interesting, but sometimes it gives very weird results like I get a revolt and end up with more troops than I started with after the battle. But I can still live with it since it's innovative.

Fourth, the fact that the order of the actions are fulfilled randomly also make it annoying because you cannot make powerful combination like move some troops on a territory to attack afterward from there. 

So there is not a very interesting strategy in completing your actions. Planning in advance is a good concept but being able to execute them in any order you want to fulfill your goals would have been better.

Finally, Castles, temples and Theaters are only used for scoring. They can have some special benefits but not all the time. And It think that sucks. I would have like buildings to have a permanent ability. Like each castle gives +1 defense, etc. So that they are needed, besides scoring to build something.

Anyways, I'll make a variant rule draft suggestion in my geek list ("board games are made to be modified").

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
This rating is fairly tentative because we played a rule wrong that made the peasants much too powerful, and also had to stop the game before its conclusion, but I think I got a good idea of the general feel of the game. I think describing it like Risk with a lot of Risk's aspects fixed is a good description. I say Risk and not some other area control game because like Risk the core mechanisms are actually fairly basic, but it feels a bit sloggy on the map and the game is quite long and supposedly epic. Like Risk the combat is fairly abstracted, but it is nice that if you get unlucky at some points, the odds are more in your favor (or vice-versa), and having more units is an advantage now without being guaranteed victory. Map is better balanced than Risk. The definite end is also good.

I like the action programming because the limited spots helps prevent too much runaway leader: you can only do so many actions a turn, whilst having more regions increases your rice upkeep and makes it more likely regions will revolt. The order things happen can also add more decision in timing things, although the technically 300 turns in a 5 player game is a bit much especially if someone isn't on top of when their turn is.

The revolt mechanism adds some interesting juggling of which region you should activate for what.

I really wish buildings had more to them, as they are 90% just different ways to score area majority. The abstraction of this game, whether via the buildings, the cubes, no thematic starting region based on history, etc. is definitely what hurts the game the most for me.

RATING: 8
This one looks like Risk (dice are replaced with a slightly more honest "army tower"), but is a completely different much more strategic game. Instead of blindly conquering territory, you need to consider food supplies and make sure you can gather enough taxes from your territories and avoid riots. The more territory you conquer, the weaker you get in those territories. And the way to gather points is by occupying (and defending) those regions that have buildings in them (not necessarily built by yourself). 
What I like is that planning your actions happens concurrently with your opponents. Still this game often goes over 2 hours play. But with the right people at the table, that is worth the sit, even though there isn't a lot of action going on on the board. 

Minus: 1 - 9

RATING: 5.9
3-5p. 150m.  I played this game once, and am unlikely to want to play again.  There is too much randomness, and added to chaos, left me feeling more like the game played me.  The first turn, I was so lost that I simply placed my face-down cards randomly.  The second turn I'd figured out what was going on, but still placed them randomly--only checking to make sure I didn't leave a bid/bluff card on the rice harvest spot.  Finally, by the 3rd turn of the game (there are only 6 in total) had enough happened to make thinking seriously about placement at all interesting.  I came in a distant 4th place of the max 5 players.

RATING: 8
7/4/07: Rating of 8 after three plays.  Impressive heavy euro game where most of the decisions are intimately tied with other decisions.  While this is true of most good euros, perhaps I notice it here as there are so many decisions to make, and the fact that the order of half of the  actions in any given turn are hidden when you plan your moves for that turn.  While not a wargame, I appreciate how this game encourages conflict for similar reasons that many real wars happen; desire for resources (in this case, for points).  All in all, a very fun game that I'm happy to play.

RATING: 6
Rating based on 4 players.

The cube tower was a fun way to resolve battles, and I like the general idea of assigning actions ahead of time (it kind of simulates the "lag" of command in the time of slower communication), but this game felt odd to me. In general, in games you have a balance between having to plan ahead and getting the information you need to make intelligent choices... but Shogun requires you to plan TEN actions ahead with very little information. This doesn't make the game random, as planning for the unexpected is part of the strategy, but to me it felt a bit unsatisfying. I found ten actions too many as well, as even when planning I just could not keep them all in short-term memory, would get AP as a result, and in the end just assign the last few actions just to keep the game going. Not a bad game, but I found it slightly frustrating.
