RATING: 7
I've played five-player games six times, and four-player four times. Note: I played it predecessor, Wallenstein, once. Shogun is a very nice game that I look forward to playing again. Though at 3 hours, probably not too often. Production values are high, the card icons fairly well unambiguous. I still wish they'd just reprinted Wallenstein in English. There are so many games set in medieval Japan and so few during the 30 Years War. Why they changed the locale is beyond me. I strongly preferred the more globular map of Germany, over this long skinny map of Japan. This venue change was enough to tip me from "buy this" to "play someone else's copy".

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Excellent game, I love it. Three small improvements from Wallenstein: 
1. transparant tower exit box so you can see the cubes that are still left over
2. the season starts with a round of bidding for cards. These cards offer small advantages and decide the turnorder
3. the event cards are open, so you don't have to memorize anymore how much grain/rice is counted down in the winter etc.
All together the randomness has decreased a bit. It is an improved version of Wallenstein, however, if you own Wallenstein already, I would not recommend buying Shogun as well.

RATING: 9
A slicker, more compact, much more intense & less random Wallenstein. Love it. There are a couple of elements in Shogun that are worth nicking & putting back into Wallenstein too. I like the Feudal Japanese theme though thematically, Wallenstein is a little more appealing in that regard.

One of the aspects I love about the game is that you have to pick your wars carefully because they are resource intensive / wasteful. But you still need to keep a tension with your opponents or they will just run away with their little economic expansion management plans, so there is an escalation thing happening as everyone keeps a military check on everyone else, but who will be the first to pull the trigger?? Or will it just be a cold war of sorts??

Love the game.

RATING: 6
One play. (Rate Wallenstein a 9)

Thoughts on differences between this and Wallenstein:

Bidding for turn order and bonuses: I enjoy the quickness and randomness of Wallenstein's system. Though it doesn't take long to choose a bonus/turn-order in Shogun, it adds negatively to an already longish game.

Components: some warping on the personal boards. The building tokens are a little too similar and "photo shoppy." Nice coloring on the map and tower. Wish they added the grain/gold numbers on the map (to improve on Wallenstein's lack).

Map: I like Wallenstein's grand open feel. The Shogun map felt a little limiting, even with sea attacks.

RATING: 7.5
1st real game played with 5 players. It's a strange game with a million decisions to make. At first it seems the game will be too short with 8 turns, then it seems that it will last forever, and then in the midst of fighting (which I discourage now) there's a measly one turn left and the game seemed to have taken just the right amount of time. 

I think my next play will be quicker, and my score better. I ended up fighting a lot and that was my downfall (that and the fact everyone else played better than I).

Oh yeah, the cube tower rocks. It provides enough tension and excitement to really carry the game.

The bits and board are of very high quality.

Update: 3 player isn't bad. I think 4 players will be its sweet spot, yet to play it that way.

RATING: 8
Game in a Nutshell: Basically be the most famous warlord in ancient Japan. Building buildings works best but taking over others land helps give an edge.

Played as: 4 player

Pros: I like how the game makes you focus on what you have going on in your own territories first then look to expand when the opportunity presents itself advantageous. And the cube tower is hands down the best way of deciding a battle ever by balancing the odds for all players from war to war by ensuring equal loses for both parties involved.

Cons: Despite everything wonderful about the game, surprisingly it felt a little dry. I never felt too much tension (though it fully had my interest).

Other: Honestly, dice battle is never going to feel the same. I also really need to play this more then once to get a better read on it.

Best Use: When you want a battle game with minimal battle but still splashes of it over a 2 hour period.

RATING: 10
Awesome game! After playing it several times then playing Risk which I've known for several years, Risk seemed like a terrible game so now it's somewhere in my cellar. Shogun is a very strategic game where - contrary to Risk - the aim isn't to control more land, though that could help, but to gain the most points. You can collect taxes and rice but beware as this might provoke a rebellion in your own province and you might end up losing it! The more provinces you control the more food you need. This makes the game extremely interesting even though it may take a lot of time to play. But it's definitely worth it!

RATING: 7
Pick actions and move your armies to improve and expand your empire for most points.

Plays: A couple?
Type: Faux wargame = a game where you move military forces on a map, but where victory isn't intrinsically about conquest or destruction of enemies.
Complexity: Medium strategy game.
# of players: 5.
Playing time: See BGG.
Player interaction: Conflict.
Randomness: Low - Cube tower.

Replayability: Low, once in a while is good enough.
Fun: Pouring armies into the cube tower is one of the funniest experiences in gaming, so don't miss out. The murderous peasants are also funny, every game will have some provinces revolt, to groans and cheers. I'm also very partial to simultaneous hidden order selection, as it keeps downtime to almost nothing and makes for difficult decisions with uncertainty taken into account.
Memorability: Decent, but the map and armies are bit too generic to really inspire stories.

Thematic integration: Decent.
Components: Iconic. The cube tower is one of a kind. The map and other components are also good.

Who will like this: For people that like multi-player strategy games with conflict, this might be the best generic one.

RATING: 7.5
Interesting worker placement/area control game. Players take on the role of Japanese warlords trying to achieve the status of Shogun. Victory points are scored for territory occupation and for having the majority of castles, temples and theatres in a province. The USP of the game is that there are 10 actions that players can take each turn, whose order is randomly determined at the start of each round. Players have to allocate an action to a territory at the start of the round, so planning is all important. The other fun and interesting aspect of the game is the Battle tower. When battles occur, participating armies (cubes) are thrown into the tower and whoever has the most exit the bottom wins. The tower itself is constructed so that some cubes remain inside it, meaning that the outcome of the conflict is never certain. The game is enjoyable, if a little overlong (2 hours+)and some players will baulk at the randomness of both the action order and particularly the battle resolution. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
The game has an awful lot of randomness, a bit much for my taste. I enjoyed the game never the less, but there's a limit to how invested I let myself get. Between the random order that the action cards come out (only the first 4 of 10 are known before you program which region does what)and the cube tower (combat is resolved by throwing the combatants in and seeing what comes out, it can be a little off-putting for me. The only time it really frustrated me was when I'd attack with, say 4 cubes against a region with 1 defender and have 1 friendly and 1 enemy cube come out, leaving no one on the board.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a retheme of one of my favorite games, Wallenstein.  I like it as much as I like the original.  There are two mechanics that make the game fun.  Of course you've got the famous combat tower, into which warring armies go, but only the ones that pop out the bottom survive.  But there's also the less-talked-about action selection mechanic which is really fun.  You can do each action (build an army, move an army, build a temple, etc) only once per turn, and each province can only perform one action.  So does Sagami build units or tax the people for money?  Should your taxation action go to Sagami or Shinto?  It's a tough game, and lots of fun.

RATING: 9
I really like this one. The cube tower makes the combat be pure luck based, but whereas in a dice game you my have a reaaally long bad luck streak, if your armies get trapped inside the tower you can count on them to appear on a future battle.

You have to calculate each turn with precision, because there are only 6 rounds to play. Very good game indeed. Even though the rules are really easy to understand it may be hard to understand at first, but after the first game I assure you'll get the grip of it.

I haven't played the expansions (waiting for the Big Box Kickstarter  to arrive ¬¬)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Welcome to the chaotic world of Feudal Japan. Choose which actions to do in which provinces without knowing the order. Balance between development and war without knowing what you will keep and what you will gain. Resolve battles by dropping your forces in a tombola without knowing what will come out when you need it the most and what will come out when you need it the least. 

Normally I shouldn't like a chaotic game like this. Yet, the misty game board in the shadow of the dreaded Cube Tower conveys a thematic fog of the war feeling. You issue orders but have little impact on their outcome. Instead, you must outwit your enemies by deploying your forces where they least expect it while also maintaining a balanced economy and keeping both soldiers and peasants happy.

Many claim that Shogun is neither a war game, nor a euro game, but I don't care - the game experience is both immersive and cutthroat and simply fantastic.

RATING: 5
Shogun is an interesting game that is made much better by the very cool cube tower and combat resolution system.  The game has a warish, conflicty feel, but it is really an area control game. Still, it's a very interesting implementation of area control, and any game that requires the pre-planning of moves goes up in my book.

And while the game has a lot of cool elements to it, it does seem to be lacking something.  Perhaps it's the constrained number of actions you will take over the course of the game.  Or perhaps that the map seems to greatly favor the players on the edges.  Either way the game is fun to play, but not one I'd really want to devote many, many plays to fully discover. I'm just not sure there is much to find.

RATING: 7
The re-themed version of Wallenstein that includes two different maps to play on. Players now bid 0 to 4 gold on player order and which special ability to use ( 1 rice,  1 gold,  1 cube when attacking,  1 cube when defending, add a cube when placing men). The cards are now easier to comprehend, since they are language independent. It basically plays the same as Wallenstein, so I don't think you need to have this new version if you have Wallenstein. It's still a 3 hour game, but enjoying, if you like the game system.

RATING: 1
Shogun has hoodwinked boardgamers into thinking it is a game. It has done so by having all the same components of the standard boardgame, and by having many of the same problems of a boardgame like a long setup time with many fiddly bits and poorly laid out instructions where  important info is difficult to look up. 

The real problem with Shogun is the way the game limits the number of each available action and how they are chosen. Basically, all players do approximately the same number of all the same things each turn, the only variation being where each action takes place. The method of choosing each location is done by each player placing a card representing a province they own on a grid of each available action, once. No one is able to devote themselves to doing more of an action than the game permits, which makes play feel arbitrarily limited. This system both removes player's agency in the game and allows the now disinterested players to play completely on auto-pilot, choosing actions by just randomly placing their cards. Worse still, players on auto-pilot actually remain competition for the players still engaged in consciously choosing where their actions occur further highlighting how the arbitrary limitations of Shogun restrict player ability to do anything meaningful.

Even Shogun's famous cube tower lends itself to this auto-piloting as combat has been boiled down to taking either player's involved armies and chucking them down the tower with the winner being decided by whoever's cubes spill out at the bottom.

In effect, all players could randomly play their cards down every turn, and watch how Shogun plays out like some kind of machine with zero player involvement. This is not a game! It is a box of parts that looks like a game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Holy cow.  The only thing holding it back from a 10 is the chintzy little cardboard cut-outs for the buildings.  How could you, Queen?  How could you?

Oh, it shaved an extra $15 off the cost?  OK.

The gameplay is exactly as good as everyone else has remarked.  It's a Euro area-control programming fest what thinks it's a GMT wargame.  There's a ton to do, too little time to do it in, plenty of conflict and fairly well-rounded conflict at that, the last season means everything's still up for grabs so kingmaking is virtually impossible, a rebalanced map makes turtling extraordinarily difficult, and the whole thing ends right before it wears out its welcome.

You have to determine what territories are critical for your victory; and while there's a handful of no-brainers the rest are anyone's guess and will depend largely on your initial position and their build-outs.  All of this means that the replay value is very high: a ramped-up version of El Grande.  Plus, for me, the Japanese Samurai theme and history crushes that of the 100 Years War.

Three years later, and this gem is still seeing the table with an enthusiastic "Yes".  If we've got 2 hours to spend on a game-night, it's up for grabs.

RATING: 3
I would rate it 6 with three players and 1 with five. In a five-player game if you get weak in the beginning (after a weak or unfortunate initial setup others can take several of your provinces) you look ahead to a minimum of three hours (the game can easily last four) of boredom, you might as well just give up your remaining provinces and leave.
It is more difficult to get weak when played by three. The game is enjoyable, but people who don't like luck in their games, should avoid it. The source of most luck is a unique cube tower that retains some cubes that were thrown in it. The color majority of cubes that come out decides the winning player in a battle. It is a faster way to decide a battle than the dice method used in risk, and the probabilities are different.

RATING: 7
心得:
主要是賣互動的遊戲
10個行動多數可以平行處理
事件好像多數沒有幫助
錢一定會不夠所有要斟酌用
總共最多12場戰鬥
但是戰鬥骰塔隨機性太高
也讓遊戲策略度稍微下降
遊戲時間過長是個問題
而遊戲內的策略面又還好
主要應該還是個嘴砲的遊戲
規則:
*先根據挑選的版圖面跟玩家挑出對應的省份卡(3人部分區域不用)，每個玩家7個方塊+10個方塊丟到骰塔，掉下來的清空回各自供應區，事件卡洗勻開四張
*3/4/5人起始18/15/12元，每個人拿個人圖版對應大名卡跟顏色方塊還有寶箱卡，分數0，食物0
*每個人先用個人圖版背面把方塊對應數目分成不同群，之後省份卡洗勻開兩張，輪流由起始玩家開始拿走一張卡(二選一 或是牌庫頂，如果有重複可移到牌庫底開新的兩張)並放一群方塊到該省份，馬上補新的卡，直到每個人全部方塊都放完
*遊戲總共進行兩年八大輪，一大輪一季，春夏秋行動輪，冬天記分輪，行動輪有六個階段
1.行動卡洗勻隨機放在版圖下，前5張打開
2.特殊卡洗勻隨機放在五個順位格(結算先結算事件卡在結算特殊卡)
3.蓋著出牌在個人圖板上(11格，1格1張)決定哪個省份做哪個動作跟競標
>花3/2/1元蓋 城堡/寺廟/劇院，同個省份不能同樣建築物，建築數量每個省份不同
>省份根據牌徵米/稅，如果有暴動標記結算戰鬥，之後再放一個暴動標記
>花3/2/1元 放 5/3/1個兵，放1兵的動作同時可移動該省份的任意個兵到擁有的鄰近省份(至少留1，不能主動放棄自己占領的地)
>戰鬥 移動該省份的兵到附近沒人或他人的省份進行戰鬥結算
*錢牌出在行動格代表不做該行動，省份牌出在競標格代表0元，但是順位比0元前面
4.決定事件 剩下的開著的洗勻抽一張成為這回合事件
5.決定順位 依競標價格拿特數卡並放大名卡成為這回合順位依據，平手隨機抽大名卡決定順位
6.執行行動 10個行動依序執行，第1個執行時開第6個，同理直到所有行動照順位執行完，行動可以執行一定要執行，資源不夠就放棄，執行完進入下季，
*第四第八輪為冬天記分輪，先根據剩下的事件卡跟擁有的省份(每省1個)每個人需支付米，不夠付的根據表格由左手玩家抽省份卡執行暴動，放置農民方塊數為該省份上暴動標記數目加上表格上額外加的農民方塊，之後記分，之後抽新的4張事件卡，米歸0，暴動標記移除，第二次記分後，平手比錢
>每個省份1分，每個建築1分，每個大區域(總共5個)最多城堡/寺廟/劇院的拿3/2/1分，平手拿2/1/0分
*戰爭發生時，該省份所有方塊跟托盤所有方塊都會丟進骰塔中，掉下來的方塊只有相關戰爭的才會結算，攻擊方跟防守方相同數量方塊移回各自供應區，剩下的才放回該省份，如果有省份平手或農民獲勝，移除該省份上所有建築物跟暴動方塊，該省份成為中立區，贏家拿走對應省份卡
*PVP 防守方如果沒有暴動標記，落下的農民算是支援防守方，農民勝利時全部方塊移回供應區，沒參與戰鬥的方塊留在托盤
*打沒人地 防守方為一個農民方塊
*徵稅或米動亂 攻擊方為暴動標記數農民方塊
*冬季戰爭 根據付不出的米數查額外增加的農民數，還有該省區的暴動標記
***兩人變體規則***
*C1棕色黃色區不用(卡片移出遊戲)，海路縮減到綠>棕變成綠>紅，紫>黃變成紫>紅
*C2 綠色區不用Iyo, Tosa, Echigo, Mutsu, Kazusa, Awa-Boso也不用，Izu to Awa-Boso > Izu to Shimosa.Tajima to Kaga > Wakasa to Noto.  
*移除+1武力特殊卡
*C1每人起始18個寶箱，遊戲進行兩年
*C2每人起始24個寶箱，遊戲進行三年
*照正常規則宣告起始領地(多一個3顆的領地，所以一開始會有10個領地)
*預設領地  在隨機或照順序選一個放3顆
C1 : 
P 1: 5 – Iyo 4 – Wakasa 4 – Ise 3 – Bingo 3 – Hida 2 – Iwami 2 – Tajima 2 – Tosa 2 – Yamato 
P 2: 5 – Omi 4 – Bitchu 4 – Awa-Shikoku 3 – Aki 3 – Tamba 2 – Hoki 2 – Kii 2 – Mino 2 – Owari
C2 : 
P 1:5 – Kii 4 – Harima 4 – Shinano 3 – Ise 3 – Kozuke 2 – Shima 2 – Hida 2 – Totomi 2 – Suruga
P 2: 5 – Kai 4 – Awa-Shikoku 4 – Omi 3 – Yamato 3 – Shimosa 2 – Wakasa 2 – Echizen 2 – Shimotsuke 2 – Musashi
*決定順位競標第一個先選一張特殊卡，在換另外一個人選，之後兩人在選一次並放大名卡決定下回合順位

RATING: 7
My comments are the same as Wallenstein, the only real difference being that the shipping routes open up more surprises than you'd expect for the geography. With only 6 turns, expansion needs to be fast and brutal to obtain the riches you need to build and grow. I like the constraints placed upon you by the pre-programming, but it still falls into the multi-player beat-up issue where you'll win if you're left alone, and if everyone is conscious of keeping the playing field level, then it becomes a bit of a lottery in the last turn. And the luck of the cube-tower adds to the lottery feel. Solid and all, not just totally engaging.

RATING: 9
This is a fantastic game. I looked around at many geeks' oppinions about it and there seemed to be a lot of debate about the kind of game this is. I don't really think that's important, you just play the game for what it is! And what the game is is high quality components mixed with a great set of rules that make this by far one of the best games in my collection. If I had to tell you what kindof game it is I'd say it is a medieval japanese set war logistics game. It's important that Japan is the set since in japanese culture the "honor" plays a large role. So battle is not all there is, in fact, bellic manouveres and assaults are fairly scars in this game, what players so is, on one hand trying to avoid defeat in case of an attack, by strategically placing their units, and at the same time rise buildings that ensure honor, thus points. This is mixed with the constant preasure of internal peasent revolts, due to tax and rice collection, needed to buy units and buildings and to feed the army. In all a very good game with a fair degree of complexity that does require some thinking ahead, good planing some strategic considerations. One of the very good ones to me!

- As always, Queen Games rulebook are rolemodel on how well rulebooks can be done. Makes the game look easy until you start playing and get aware of the many tough decisions, but luckilly, the rule book helped!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This multiplayer wargame has basically the same rules as Wallenstein.  It involves pre-determining which actions to take (and where to take them) simultaneously at the start of every turn.  You earn a limited amount of money during the game, and have to determine whether to spend it on armies or on buildings, which earn you victory points but can be conquered by other players.  The dice found in most games of this type are replaced by a cube tower, which basically acts as a randomizer with memory, so if you get screwed one time, you'll be more likely to benefit later.  Unfortunately, the map in this game (unlike Wallenstein) encourages players to consolidate toward either end of the board, where many of their opponents will likely not even be able to attack them.  Oh, and I never really liked multiplayer wargames to begin with.

(1 play)

***

Strange - SHOGUN is a very good game but I thought that WALLENSTEIN was spectacular.

The rules are not that different. But the two sister-games feels different when playing.

I did not think that the bidding for turnorder brought that much to the party.

SHOGUN felt a lot more difficult to play. Maybe due to the - for me at least - new, and sometimes similar sounding japanese province names? I mixed up the provinces all the time. Also the provinces felt smaller, thus making it harder to see the cities when the provinces had a lot of armies on them. The types were clearer also in WALLENSTEIN. 

The peasant-rules were better in SHOGUN, but the artwork, the shape of the map, the events was better in WALLENSTEIN. Did feel that SHOGUN's pretty theme also felt a lot more generic than WALLENSTEIN's, it looks like the feudal japanese setting is fairly common (Samurai Sword, Samurai, Senji). It would have been interesting to have a short historic summary of the warlords on the individual payer boards.

This 8 feels very priliminary. I know SHOGUN "ought" to be better when I rated WALLENSTEIN a 10. I will of course give it some more plays, but I guess theme means a great deal to me.

RATING: 7
Amazingly light for such a large game. After you get used to all the different types of cards (won't take long), you are done with learning.
And if somebody tells you that you should concentrate your manpower to a good area and start expanding from there... believe him.

There is a lot to do, but the main point is fighting - and that's where it goes wrong for me. I liked the cube-tower idea, but it just doesn't work. Throw cubes into a blocked tower and watch what comes out. Of course you can increase your probabilities but still, it's a bit useless imho. Well, okay, it's definitely better than any combat card system would've been.

Oh well, maybe I'll get used to the tower after playing more. I hope I do, because this game has possibilities to become eight. They've done good job capturing the Japanese world onto board.

Two years (six turns + two winters) seem short at first, but it's enough. At the end I hoped that it would be a bit shorter. Winter is really harsh and unpredictable. It can be either good or bad, depends.

I see eight coming, just give me more chances to play this!

RATING: 7
In this game you get a hand of cards that match the areas you control. On your player board you have card slots that match the different actions that happen each round. During the preparation phase, you program those actions by placing the region cards on them, meaning the action will happen in the region you assign to the slot. The order of actions is randomized, and half of them are public knowlage at the start of the round. The rest get revealed as they happen. The game can feel complicated, but it flows smoothly as most of the actions can be resolved simultaneously. Combat is quick and interesting, as it involves dumping everyone's cubes into a tower and whomever has more spill out is the winner. The cost is that for every one of the defender's cubes that came out, the winner will also lose that many cubes. Cubes that retain in the tower will help you in your future battles. In this way you achieve a semi-random combat system, but the victum of this round's randomness has an advantage in future battles.

The one complaint about this game is the thing that makes it the most interesting. It feels like a dudes on the map game, on shuffle mode. You know where you want your actions to happen, but maybe someone takes that spot out from under you before it resolves. Maybe the order in which the actions happen makes it so that you can't afford the action you selected. Maybe the amount of grain needed to feed your spots at the end of the round is more than you planned for, and the peasants revolt causing you to lose spots. Maybe every combat you get into, both players tie and the spot gets wiped including the high value buildings you spent time making. I go back and forth being thrilled and frustrated.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
A little bit better that Wallenstein, mostly through the bidding for turn order. Lots of room for thought to try to make the best of the Action Order while wary of the Event card to come AND the plans of the other players. Not too successful so far with this system but it seems to require building armies in the first half (and a few well shielded/profitable buildings) and conquering other provinces containing buildings in them during the second half. I need to be more aggressive and hope that no one forgets to truly ascertain who the leader really is.

I think this is really a 4-player game to avoid 2-on-1 situations. I also find the combined randomness of hidden-Action, the timing of the Events cards, and the Tower to be excessive. Play conservatively and get an average score (if you're lucky) or go for it and trust as much to luck as skill. This could easily slip down to a 6.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.6
This is one of the best area control games I've had the pleasure of playing.   Normally I abhor randomness in long, thoughtful games but Shogun's cube tower is the perfect marriage between the unpredictability of war, and the determinism of strategic planning. 
The mechanic by which players program their actions is also a stroke of total genius. It's rare to find a game in which simply planning one's turn can be such a brain burning and brutal exercise.

It is also worth mentioning that this feels like an entirely different game at every player count, all of which are great.   At five people Shogun is a predictably frenetic free-for-all war game where players are constantly dancing around one another, hungrily expanding their borders by any means.
But at three, the game is a tense and calculative civilization builder.  Since more of the map is available, large empires quickly arise... but it is difficult to feed so many provinces. Players are quickly forced to weigh the benefits of expansionism against the need to protect themselves from against their own people.

RATING: 10
The best area control&strategy game I have played. The theme really fits here even though it's a euro. There are only two kind of attacks in this game: stupid and wise. You have to know when it's the right time to attack. You can't push your luck too hard or you have to pay the price. Careful planning is the key to victory. You must react fast to every move of your enemies. You have to know which of the provinces you need to defend and the provinces that aren't important to you. Cube tower adds memory to battles that your bad luck in one battle may very well change the odds in another battle. Faster, more fun and less luck depended in the long run than a set of dice. Probably some people try to blame the cube tower for their bad luck. Still in every game that I have played, the best player has won the game.

RATING: N/A
Based on the [gameid=3307] game system. The game is set in the Sengoku period (approx 1467-1573) which ends with the inception of the well-known Tokugawa Shogunate.  The game covers 2 years.  The rounds consist of Spring, Summer, Fall and then Winter is a scoring round each year.  There is a very limited amount of time to tax and build castles, temples, and theaters.  You must also feed your people and build up your armies to raid surrounding territories.  Watch out for the peasant revolts.  Includes a a Combat Tower!!  Each combatant gathers up all the army cubes involved, tosses them into the top of the tower.  Some will get stuck in the tower (waiting for future battles) and the rest will tumble out. The player with the most armies tumbling out wins the combat.  Not as random as rolling the bones, but just enough luck to keep it interesting.

RATING: 7.72
Not really a territory conquest game based on the original Shogun (from which it stole the name, that game is now called Samurai Swords). This is actually a points scoring Euro based on majority control in various regions. However, it has a combat interaction. That combat interaction is what really makes the game. Not only is the combat element fairly clever but it also has the dice tower. This is a large column into which you drop cubes. Some cubes drop out, some become stuck and fall out at a future resolution. It randomises individual results while ensuring a relatively equitable long term position, and even permitting forward planning.

RATING: 7
We've had this game for a while - my wife got it very cheap (I think), along with a load of other Queen games, for kickstarting Urbanisation - but we only had our first play in November 2013. In fact, we abandoned it after only 1 of the 2 "years", but this was not through disliking the game. It was purely that it had taken us 2 hours (not that it felt like it was being so slow while playing), and we decided we wanted to play some other non-ridiculously-short games. We all agreed that we'd play it again some other time soon, now we have a better idea what we're doing. However, it was nearly a year later (October 2014) that we finally played our first (and so far only) full game.

Personally, I like most of the mechanics. The cube tower is funky and fun to use, although I don't think it's crucial to the game - more of a gimmick, as you could just decide the battles with dice instead (although then you wouldn't get the odd result of sometimes ending up with more cubes in a territory after the battle than you sent in in the first place!). The mechanics of blind bidding and blind action-selection seemed very interesting, quite unfamiliar-seeming but straightforward and providing interesting decisions. I can easily see why this is in the BGG top 100. However, I don't think it's a game that I'm ever going to personally request. My concerns are that the scoring seems very uninteresting in relation to the rest of the game (although I can see how it might lead to exciting games - you want to both control lots of territories and build lots of buildings, but if you spread too thinly you will lose some territories, and therefore some of the buildings which do nothing for you except during scoring rounds, with the exception of some of the event card effects), and I can see AP being a *huge* issue for experienced players, especially towards the end of the game.

Still a very interesting game though - quite unlike any others I know.

RATING: 9
Achei um jogaço. 2 coisas me chamaram a atenção:

1. O mecanismo de resolver batalhas com a torre é genial. Muito divertido, imprevisível no ponto certo. Teve uma batalha épica entre Henrique e Santiago com 22 cubos sendo jogados na torre que chamamos até a Pati no quarto pra ver.

2. A resolução de ações é uma sacada muito boa tb. A cada turno, a gente sorteia em que ordem as dez ações vão acontecer, sendo que as 5 últimas vão sendo reveladas só depois. Vendo a ordem, vc define secretamente qual província sua vai fazer qual ação. A ordem é crucial, pois a ação de batalha pode rolar antes da de pegar reforços, ou as que gastam dinheiro antes da de pegar dinheiro. Tudo isso muda suas prioridades.

A pancadaria entre jogadores é relativamente light, pq se expandir muito custa muito caro, mas é importante não virar um alvo muito apetitoso para os outros.

O ponto negativo pra mim foi a resolução das revoltas. Como tem mais de um tipo de revolta, a regra é um pouco complicada, tinha que ficar sempre consultando o manual dependendo do caso. Outro ponto negativo (menor) é o setup chato.

A partida durou 3h30, mas éramos 5, todos jogando pela primeira vez. Acho que a próxima pode durar uma hora a menos fácil.

RATING: 8
Sort of a mix between El Grande and Game of Thrones.  The cube tower is awesome - far superior mechanic to standard dice rolling.  My main gripe is that I wish there were some way for players to select their actions without putting them face-down, either with a screen, Memoir 44-like card holders, something that would allow everyone to secretly select which province will take which action without constantly having to check what you put (or calculating exactly which ones going to go where before placing any of them down). Also, because there are quite a bit more unknowns when determining actions than AGoT for example (what's the possible order of actions, what's going to get attacked first, will I have enough money at the time each action occurs, what might the event card be and how should I optimally collect money or attack, how am I doing on rice), Shogun is more AP-prone.  The rice mechanic felt tacked on (no where near as good as the supply mechanic in AGoT) and unnecessary.  Lastly, I would like to see scoring after every turn or every two turns, rather than just twice in the game - that would make it more dynamic and provide players more focus on who is in the lead.  These are just some thoughts - it is still a very good game that I am happy to play, but I feel there are some clear ways in which it could be streamlined/improved.

Update: I liked this one more the 2nd time.  It can still be AP-prone but I can see this one speeding up to under 2 hours once we're all more familiar with it.  I understand better how the rice dynamic helps integrate the revolting farmers and make it more expensive to control lots of territory, but it's still feels clunky.  Looking forward to more plays, although I think I still prefer the elegance of El Grande (or Quebec), which really feels quite similar, minus the amazing cube tower.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Dudes-on-a-map game where the main mechanic is programming your turn by assigning provinces to distinct actions without knowing the exact order in which the actions will occur, and over-taxing regions results in peasant revolts.  Combat is a tower where you physically dump in the armies, and whoever comes out fight to the death, with some getting stuck in the tower and stragglers from past fights wandering into the action, which is goofy, but it works, and long term you get back out what you put in.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The only difference between this and Wallenstein is that you "bid" for turn order. Instead of the 5 blank action cards, you get 1 blank, 1 with 1 gold on it, 1 with 2 gold on it, 3, and 4. When placing actions for that round, there is an extra spot on the map for you to place one of these cards (or a province card for a bid of .5) for turn order. Before the round starts, everyone reveals these, pays the amount to the bank, and whoever bid the highest chooses first, ties broken randomly.

The twist is, the turn order also gives you a special "power" for that round. There are 5 special power cards that are shuffled and placed on the turn order track, when you get to choose one (from the bidding mentioned earlier) you exchange your personal player card for the special power card, thus earning you a special power and a spot on the turn track. The special powers are:  1 Attack,  1 Defense,  1 Gold,  1 Rice,  1 Unit when completing the 3 for 5 action.

It really doesn't make a whole lot of difference in the game, but it does add a little something extra. Overall; same experience, same feeling, same strategies, same game.

Best with 3 as an economic game.

RATING: 8.5
Shogun is an area control game wherein players gain points for provinces, buildings, and having a majority of a building type in a region.  The game goes through 2 years, with 4 seasons each, 2 seasons set aside as scoring rounds.  The other 6 seasons players each get 10 actions, varying from building, deploying new troops, gathering rice and money, and attacking.  Players assign a province to an action, and a province can only act once per season, this causes players to carefully consider what they want to do each round, and what you think your opponents will do.  The order of the 10 actions is randomized each season, and only 5 are revealed during the planing phase.  Each season also has an event card with it which is revealed after players decide their turn, and this can radically change who can be attacked, how much food or taxes you can collect, and other modifiers.

The game is not a total-domination warfare game as players do have to supply their provinces with food during the scoring round or risk a farmer revolt, which could cause the province and all its buildings to be lost.  So players must keep their expansionism in check.  Also, farmers will revolt if you take food and/or taxes from them more than once per year, and since each province gives out a different value of food and money players have to carefully consider how to fund their war while keeping the farmers from revolting, or having enough army presence to put down a revolt.

Battles are accomplished via a cube tower in which the cubes are place in the top and some come out while others stay in.  The game is pre-seeded with 7 armies from each player and 10 farmers, so battles are somewhat random, adding tension.  A smaller force can defeat a larger force, and a player can end up with more armies than they started with.  This allows for some level of chance and keeps the battles interesting as the outcome is never known ahead of time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
One of my all-time favorites. Easy to learn, but requires considerable thinking for solving the puzzle of assigning orders to provinces each turn. As most action programming games, there's a lot of double-guessing the plans of your opponents. The luck is there and it is possible to lose because of bad battle results against the odds, but the dice-tower does a good job of evening it out and good play is much more important.

Because initial position is of paramount importance, I always pick between three face-up cards instead of two during the set-up: it still makes for a different game each time, but in practice eliminates the problem of drawing particularily unlucky positions, while at the same time making it harder to attain exclusive ownership of a region, resulting in even chances for everybody.

RATING: 7
I had originally feared that wooden cubes were for pussies. Only thing that was a pussy in Shogun was me when attack showed up before taxation, as I trembled at the prospect of hungry enemies taking apart my regions before I could exploit them. This is a mean, nasty game for [u]real men[/u].

It's Risk for adults. You have to watch how far you stretch yourself, because you don't just have to deal with retaliations, but also with upkeep and possible revolts. You have to watch out if you build VP-generating castles, because that's just disturbing that hornet's nest of other players. You have to watch the order the actions come up and plan ALL 11 actions carefully, lest that cutting off of your supply handicaps you for the rest of the year (half of the game).

And conflict is delicious, courtesy of the cube tower. The way it works makes you feel consoled if a battle tips its favor against you, because you know the odds will favor you in future battles. It's just a great no-hassle way to resolve combat, without pointing fingers and mistakenly thinking that it's just an unlucky day for you. I love conflict in games, but Shogun makes me shit-ass scared of them because of the simultaneous orders. Tension is high, and players get pissed! This is what multiplayer conflict is about!

This is an 8 after one play, though I suspect it could go higher. The drawbacks that restrain me are the inherent headache with planning 11 actions at once (analysis paralysis and getting caught in shitty situations which you could admittedly avoid), and there are A LOT of things to take into account of so I don't know if I should just go with my gut feeling, or plan for everything and fail. I really wish that each region had its vital statistics printed on the map in addition to being on the cards and player aid.

Right now, if I want to spend 2-3 hours kicking some ass with people and getting a satisfying headache in the process, I will play Shogun. The game is satisfying, because the board changes so much, whole armies vanquish or disappear, and it's amazing to see how the castles and temples direct all the action like an inept traffic controller (since it leads all armies to collide in one nuclear explosion). I believe that given time and more plays, Shogun will be my ass-kicking game of choice.

[u]UPDATE[/u]: The really disappointing thing is Shogun takes far too long with newbies, especially for a game with this much chaos. Sorely needs a timer, which could create more mistakes that make the game more fun. Really don't want to play this with AP-prone people. Have to try it with 3.

A little too chaotic for a 3-hour game.

RATING: 6
Shogun -- Played once; four-player.  

The variable phase order and the players' foreknowledge of only the first phases when they plan their actions, make for interesting game flow. 

The tower mechanism is really cool.  While it seems limited to conflict-based uses, I can imagine game designers working this mechanism into new games, as it provides a more balanced randomness than dice.  

It seems to me that Shogun's primary thrust is area control.  So it seems odd that there are so few opportunities to invade others' territories -- two attack actions per season, for six seasons, for a total of twelve opportunities in all.  If area control was simply one among many strategies in Shogun, then the dearth of invasion actions might be understandable.  But that's simply not the case -- area control is at the heart of the game.   

In the end, despite the really cool tower, we won't be purchasing Shogun.  There are a number of other area-control games I'd rather play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I like Shogun. A friend dubbed it "Risk on acid", but the resemblance to Risk is fairly specious. Shogun is an excellent strategic game of conflict, however, which also does an outstanding job of incorporating role selection, blind bidding, bluffing, and even negotiation - to some degree. At least, Shogun allows for negotiation. I wouldn't say the game design promotes it. Some have remarked that playing the two years (six actual full turns and two scoring turns) does not allow the game to develop as fully as players would like. And I agree that going for three or four years would provide an interesting experience. The problem with this is simply a matter of time. After playing a one year trial game, our first full game still took four hours. I would say that the players were neither exceptionally fast nor slow, but average with respect to play time. It didn't *seem* like four hours to me, but that is because there is very little down time in Shogun, were you are just twiddling your thumbs. The time-consuming part of the game is the act of secretly matching up your provinces with the 10 actions which occur every turn. Some might call this downtime, but it's really sort of a mutual brain melt. Each player is examining the board, assessing the possible events for the season, the five revealed actions, and their own position - trying to figure out the most advantageous way to appropriate the actions. This takes time, as so much rides upon the decisions made.

The one thing I dislike about Shogun is that the weight given to this process of matching roles to provinces is disproportionate to the amount of planned strategic impact which results from the process. What you end up doing, and when, and where - IS THE GAME. But because the process is blind, and because you only see the order of the first five action types for the season, you have to spend a lot of time speculating as to what your opponents are doing in order to decided what you are going to do (and when and where during the season). This all has a tremendous impact on the game, and everyone knows it, which is why making the choices take so long, and why the entire game ends up taking 3  hours. But it feels to me as if there is a disconnect between investment and outcome. I think simplifying this process would have improved the game - perhaps reducing the number of roles/phases down to five somehow. This is my only gripe with the game (besides the warping player mats.)

The cube tower is used to resolve combat - you can see pictures of it here. I think it works quite nicely, and helps generate tension as mild surprises can occur. You aren't merely attacking provinces in Shogun. In fact, I would say that the heart of the game is not attacking (although that may be the most *fun*), but rather positioning and strategic building. Turn order (along with a special season-specific privilege) is determined by a blind bidding process. It makes sense. Shogun is a lot of fun, but it does seem to me to be a more contemplative, slower paced game. As with many, I prefer the Moon side of the board. I recommend the game, but make sure you don't mind longer games, and that you set aside adequate time to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The game system, as far as I'm concerned, is Wallenstein perfected: I can't think of a more perfect game in the Risk-like conquest genre.  You can't just plunder your competitors: you must FEED and GARRISON your territory just to protect it from your own citizens!  More subtly, you must spread your holdings out to make the most points, penalizing those who content themselves with an invincible little corner of the board.  Folks, it doesn't get any better than this!  The only downside is that the cards and cubes like to slip and skid on the board, making it a bit tricky to handle.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Shogun is a cleverly designed and truly innovate war game, based upon the recipe from Wallenstein (only moved from Germany to Japan). In the game, players take control of a clan of samurais that are fighting for control over Japan. This is done through moving troops, harvesting resources and, hopefully, winning wars against one's opponents. 

What makes this game (these games) innovative is it's action selection mechanism and it war system.

Each round consists of ten actions (in random order), and players are secretly selecting in which areas of the map they want to conduct each of them. As such, the game is won by outmaneuvering one's opponent by swing through their plans and chose actions that them from being fulfilled. Wars, on the other hand, is determined by throwing cubes (representing armies) into a cube tower, which will determine it's outcome.

RATING: 8
I thought this was a wargame; until I played it.  It is a Euro with conflict pure and simple.  The cube tower is awesome; by simply dropping cubes in the tower battles are won and lost, casualties are assessed, and the tower is seeded for future endeavors.  Te "fairness" of the system becomes evident as poor performance in one battle is often rewarded later.  Kudos to the designer on that one.  The rest of the game is a funny little exercise in balancing production of food and gold evenly to prevent uprisings.  The area control mechanic encourages aggression but the Euro shows its colors by awarding VP's based on control of temples, theatres and castles that players can build.  A solid game I would readily play and recommend.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
You know, I was kind of expecting more out of this game.  And on my first play-through I was quite impressed with all the different elements that were coming together to create the game (not to mention all the very nice physical components and the famous and oh-so-brilliant cube tower).  But after a couple of games, I found myself feeling like Shogun was fairly linear and fairly static, and that I was very much at the mercy of luck for much of it.  I found it to be linear because the order of events and the specific special events are decided for you each turn.  At times, rather than me playing the game, it felt like the game was playing me.  I found it to be static because there is simply not enough time in a game to build up some armies, march them across the board, and launch some surprise attacks on your opponents.  The luck elements could probably be reduced by showing the order of all 10 actions and the special event for the season before making your action selections, although some people might enjoy keeping these a surprise.  For the medieval Japan theme, I still much prefer the old classic "Shogun" (AKA "Samurai Swords").  All that being said, I do enjoy this game and I find myself wondering if there is more depth of strategy than first meets the eye.  I guess I'll just have to play it a few more times to find out.  Also, because my wife enjoys this game so much, I've gone to considerable effort to put together a very playable and very customizable 2-player version: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/322141.  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 3.5/5  Total: 7.5/10

RATING: 5
As far as I'm concerned, this is "Cube Tower: The Game." I didn't care about much else in the experience; it's a fairly straightforward resource-gathering meets Risk experience. Not to say that it's not good, I think there's a lot of strategy in this and a good mix of randomness with hidden information, bluffing, and careful forethought. But it, overall, wasn't my cup of tea; I frankly suck at Risk and all games like it, and I don't have much of an investment in feudal Japan, so the theme didn't do anything for me. But the cube tower is quite possibly one of the coolest game gimmicks ever. We were having huge battles just for the pure joy of dumping giant numbers of cubes in that thing and watching what happened. I would probably play this again, just to play with the tower. So, well done to whoever though that one up.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An excellent Euro that contains that rarest of features in such games - direct conflict. The battle system, which involves dropping cubes representing army units into a tower and seeing how many drop out of the bottom, may appear to be gimmicky at first but in actual fact works very well and is a refreshing alternative to rolling dice to determine conflict results. I also like the fact that each turn you have 10 actions you can perform and all players decide in which regions these will take place in simultaneously. This means that Shogun stands apart from other area control/conflict games because you don't have attritional situations with players placing all their pieces in one area which often prevents the game from progressing. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
A dry euro, you don't feel the Japanese daimyo rivalization theme at all. But the most important, it's really too random for a game of such a calibre. You are making your decisions before knowing the turn order, before knowing the order of your own orders and before knowing the event for the season. More, the mechanics of choosing the actions simultaneously is not working well here (I prefer how it is done in e.g. Dungeon Lords, despite some weaknesses of this game). It takes too long (10 actions to allocate) and you have too scarce info when deciding. Not for me.

RATING: 7
A beautifully crafted area control game (it's not a wargame!). I've had a game take 4 hours inc. teaching 2 newbies but it was completely worth it. It usually clocks in around 2 hours.

Stunning, I love the tower mechanism to resolve wars and this has worked it's way right to the top of my 'Want in Trade' list.

It is dangerous to be in first place at the end of year one as that player almost always gets wiped out. Beyond that frustrating wrinkle, it's a great game.

Update: The board is annoying in that those on the fringes are all but guaranteed to win. Those stuck in the middle are doomed to have two or three fronts at all times. The recommended set-up dooms player 5, I think. The (new) Wallenstein map is much better.

Unsurprisingly, the game does also suffer from the AT requirement of having the players balance it; if a player is doing badly, he can be eliminated by another player.

Rating down from an 8 to a 7.

Recieved as a gift from Nato, Kate, Conrad and Henrik.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
OK, let me get my quibbles out of the way first. The cube tower can be horribly frustrating when hordes of revolting farmers wait until your turn to hurl themselves from the cube tower, randomly obliterating your army and province while everyone else skates free and laughs. And Shogun, like many area-control games, has a ton of bash-the-leader and king-maker opportunities - eventualities, really - which both are typically turn-offs for me. But ... so many other things shine here that the quibbles are no more than that: minor chinks in the armor of a champion. Every action is critical in Shogun, and each action/province assignment is an agonizingly, wonderfully difficult decision. Strong planning, strategy and tactics are all rewarded. Shogun is deep, but also does not overstay its welcome in terms of game length. Best of all, while being deep and challenging, Shogun has some real historical flavor and a high fun factor. Perhaps not perfect, but certainly one of the best Euro war/area control games out there!

RATING: 6
Not a big fan of this game.  But I know people that love it so I'll give it a 6.  I like games with simple mechanics and subtle strategies.  This game, I feel, has very complicated mechanics and planning each round is tortuous for me.  I really suck at this game and don't really enjoy playing.  I just recently gave it another try and was reminded why it had been so long.

This is definitely a "screw your neighbor game" because players commonly attack each other.  That is why so many people compare this with Risk but it's really nothing like Risk.  Risk is all about attacking each other non-stop with the goal of world domination.  This game is really more about maximizing victory points and trying to minimize the risks of losing important provinces to other players.

I just have a real tough time with this game.  The planning period of each round, I find to be the epitome of "analysis paralysis".  Not my cup of tea at all.

RATING: 8
This game has good "genes" to begin with. Wallenstein, though somewhat aged, is one of the most innovative games in the genre. That innovative even I haven't seen its mechanics being reiterated in another game. Really, the Dice tower and the altering action sequence partly hidden and partly known are two unique features I haven't seen adapted in other boardgames. On top, the reimplementation has awesome graphics but it lacks the history almanach that Wallenstein had included. A little disappointment there but nothing too serious. What I didn't like so much is an issue I see with every game that includes a considerable chunk of confrontational nature.It's just not made to be played with 3, even though the game's still fun! Too much "Crush the middle"-effect. Even more so with a "corridorous" map such as this. But again, I'd love to play again with 4 or 5!

RATING: 6
Not a bad game overall. I like the variable phase order and the way you play hidden cards.

The area control part is just not very good. You can only attack with two different armies each round, and you can't increase an army and attack with them in the same round. This is an intentional limitation but it really feels too limiting. It also ensures that games will ususually play out the same way, with people spending the first few rounds building up their armies then attacking weaker areas later in the game.

For me, Chaos in the Old World is a much better, much more fun game. Eight-Minute Empire: Legends is also a better game with a similar feel if you only have three or four players, although on a smaller scale.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Overall, this (and Wallenstein, of course) is a well-designed game. Each round you have 10 possible actions, assigning, at most, 1 province to each, which makes for some difficult choices.

The real hero though for this game is the combat tower. I know it has some critics out there, but it's just fun.

Though while I enjoy this game, I don't see it hitting the table often due to its length. It took us almost 3 hours, not including the rules explanation or drafting (which we skipped). I see that time coming down a little bit, but not a lot. The only drag on time was the planning phase, and while some players took longer than others, no one seemed to take an unreasonable amount of time. It's just a long game.

Also, there's a considerable amount of chaos created by the seasonal events, random action order (the last half being hidden), and occasional loss of actions due to battle losses. I think these work well for a game designed to have conflict, but I personally prefer less chaos when a game is this long.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Japan during the Sengoku or “Warring States” Period: each player assumes the role of a great Daimyo with all his troops. Each Daimyo has the same 10 possible actions to develop his kingdom and secure points. To do so he must deploy his armies with great skill. Each round, the players decide which of the actions are to be played out and in which of their provinces. If battle ensues between opposing armies, the unique Cubetower plays the leading role. The troops from both sides are thrown in together and the cubes that fall out at the bottom show who has won immediately. Owning provinces, temples, theaters, and castles means points when scores are tallied. Whichever Daimyo has the highest number of points after the second tally becomes – SHOGUN!

RATING: 7
I would like to rate it, but we played one thing wrong.  It wasn't a huge mistake, but I think it had a big impact on my opinion of the game.  We played so that in order to skip an action you put a war chest on it, not just a card.  We got the money back, but this had several effects.  1) Everyone knew what you were skipping.  2)  You didn't lose the money, but it tied the money up and made a tight economy even tighter.  3)  Since you often couldn't afford to tie up money, we were encouraged to take more actions than we wanted.  When I played, I felt that the economy was too tight, especially since it seemed to encourage you to do more than you wanted to.  This one change would probably have a significant impact on my opinion.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A true masterpiece. The genius of this design is in how Dirk Henn packed in so much great randomness and chaos without the game spinning out of control. The balancing mechanisms are clever and organic. The cube tower hurts now, but helps later. Take too many provinces and you suffer in the winter grain revolts. Every season offers so many great decisions and trade offs to ponder, as you must always consider your opponents' likely actions alongside your own.

I have only played the original Wallenstein on Spielbyweb, but I think the bidding for turn order and special powers, and the new seasonal events are a great improvement. I like the new map as well, even though it is a little easier to get isolated, isolation is often not a great advantage.

RATING: 8

This game would easily get rating 9, if it would not take so much time, space and commitment of players, but if those are arranged, count me in.

Hmm, yeah, take strategic suggestions from guy who has played the game twice! But still, it is better than taking hints from players who have played just one half game. ;) I won the first game years ago when I concentrated on building, the second time I chose the path of war... On the third game I was forced to the war...

Updated after fifth play, I concentrated on the peace again and this time I could build my defenses and keep the peace and win the game. Great game.

RATING: 9
FINALLY got to play this... and it's an insta-fave. Possibly a 10, I'll have to play at least once more to make sure. 

The limited action pool similar to Game of Thrones or Forbidden Stars, the special cards for each province similar to TI3's planets, classic area control like Risk, and of course, the Cube Tower! All of these things together is like blending parts of all of my favorite games, along with a theme I particularly like. I enjoyed it as much or even more than I expected. 

Initial potential issues: It's fairly difficult to spread out and conquer, it seems to be more about small expansion, then building and defending your buildings. I would enjoy some way to negate such a difficult "feed your people" phase... but revolts aren't THAT bad anyway I guess. 
It's a bit swingy, but few area control games aren't anyway.
It feels fairly complete with no expansions... I'm curious but worried about what they could add that didn't feel bloated or tacked on. 
I sort of wish there was a slight alteration to the cube tower to make it less direct to drop in the top (A small chute?) but this is not a real problem. 

Anyway, If I have a 2nd game that was as fun as my first, I'll bump this to a 10. I only hope expansions make it even better rather than distract.

RATING: 5
The cube tower mechanism is definitely cool, and certainly more strategic (and, yes, more random) than a simple dice roll, but that alone won't carry the game.  The nifty way that everything else comes together, though, does.  The special action/turn order thing is nice, as are the multiple functions of the cards (both in your hand/board and the action deck).  Additionally, the way that players play their cards face down, and the way that they can be stolen, is pretty cool, too.  Oh, and that you'll only be able to do one action per region each turn is a nice balancing and frustrating (but in a good way) mechanism.  That it's not an overly complex game, but is still a great challenge, is indicative of its lasting appeal.  This is a game deserving of its rating.

1 play: 4 players

RATING: N/A
Shogun is based on the Wallenstein game system. The game is set in the Sengoku period (approx 1467-1573) which ends with the inception of the well-known Tokugawa Shogunate.
\13\\13\
Japan during the Sengoku or “Warring States” Period: each player assumes the role of a great Daimyo with all his troops. Each Daimyo has the same 10 possible actions to develop his kingdom and secure points. To do so he must deploy his armies with great skill. Each round, the players decide which of the actions are to be played out and in which of their provinces. If battle ensues between opposing armies, the unique Cubetower plays the leading role. The troops from both sides are thrown in together and the cubes that fall out at the bottom show who has won immediately. Owning provinces, temples, theaters, and castles means points when scores are tallied. Whichever Daimyo has the highest number of points after the second tally becomes – SHOGUN!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Strategy Games Top 100
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is a resource management game with a feudal Japanese war game theme.  There are ten actions that can be performed each turn and players must limit each action to one province.  This throttles back the overwhelm-your-opponent strategy I dislike in other dice-based wargames.  Not to mention that this game doesn't use dice, but a randomizing tower in which armies go in and may come out or may stay behind for a later fight.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Area control of Japan set during the Sengoku period of warring states. This game has systems that I really enjoy and don't see in many(any?) other strategy area control games. Love how the setup phase is a sub game of initially placing control cubes from turns of drawing and selecting from the territory cards. The next phase has simultaneous programmed actions (revealed and hidden) which are played on your available actions board to build, muster, attack, get money or grow rice and often involve great anguish. I dig the interaction of this phase trying to second guess opponents and love the added bluff action cards dynamic in strategy! Plus, winning combat in this game means something. You claim the opponents region card---that is big! Don't forget to feed your peasants in winter...riots can be bad. I'm on the fence with the cube tower. I don't often play pure war/area control games but when I do it's Shogun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Fantastic - big fan of this game. Great solo rules available as well.

RATING: 10
Gaming masterpiece.  As warlords in 16th-century Japan, players attempt to secure a position of dominance for their respective clans.  The most successful warlord at the end of the game will become Shogun.  

Players all choose their various actions simultaneously and then resolve them in turn order, so there is very little down-time.  To succeed, a player will need not only to control as many provinces as possible, but also to develop his realm by building castles, temples, and theaters.  The player who manages to build the most buildings in each of the 5 regions will be rewarded with valuable bonus points.

RATING: 5
You shuffle things around on a board, construct Things, and skirmish occasionally.

Contrary to popular nuttiness, the inclusion of a dice tower is neither a necessary nor a sufficient condition for quality of play.  It can enhance the gaming experience - Dragon Land is a good example - but it won't turn a dry sponge into buttermilk biscuit.

Update:

On a deeper reflection of Shogun's mechanics, I've come to a self-realization that I like pre-planning all my moves equally as much as I like pre-planning my entire life - which is to say, not at all.

RATING: 5
Despite the hidden allocation the game tends to checkmate at the multiplayer intersections and turn into an attrition shoving match at the two player points. As with most multiplayer lines of battle games, only with the right (i.e. all good at reading the board) group of players willing to play the diplomacy alliance game to reduce the fronts does the game get good. 

Unfortunately the linear nature of the island makes the end spot empires too safe and the spots right next to the end spot empires puts you in the role of spoiler where you alone can attack the winning player and need to at the same time persuade the other players to leave you alone so that you can attack the winning player.

RATING: 7
This is one of the earlier War/Euro hybrids and it is a really neat game.  I like the fog of war of planning for a whole round of moves while only knowing the order of half of the actions and found the cube tower entertaining if less predictable than most any other combat system I've dealt with.  I even found the risk/reward of revolts in provinces you repeatedly tax to be an interesting thing to manage.  I did feel like the random round effecting cards after you've carefully planned your moves to be one random factor too far... but I didn't think it was a game breaker as gambling on what might come up could be exciting too.  I think I'd really enjoy this a lot with the right group.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
Fantastic game design here!  Every session is fraught with tough decisions, which (when made correctly) are rewarded come the Winter scoring round.

This is the kind of game where a well-thought-out play will draw comments of apprectiation from your opponents, even if they just got creamed... which is one of the best things you can say about a game. Plus, given the difficulty level, there's a real sense of accomplishment when things are going well. Like all the best games, winning a game of Shogun is something you can be proud of...and which can't easily be chalked up to luck.

Every turn is a social event.  In-game interaction between players isn't at an all-time high (as in games with more conflict) but each outcome is cause for comment, discussion, laughter, comiseration, elation, dejection... all like you said ("I'll give you the 10-peso version").

The only thing holding me back from rating this game higher is... the decisions REALLY are agonizing.  It's hard work sometimes, balancing the needs of your empire with the need to expand/score more points. I think I'll just about always want to play... but the game seems a tiny bit more -challenging- than it is fun.  It's only off by an inch, but it'll never be my favorite game, for this reason.

Don't get me wrong, I love high-strategy games... and I like tough decisions... but after a good three hours (avg. for us) of Shogun, your brain'll need to cool for a bit.  Preferably over food.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I don't know about Shogun (or Wallenstein for that matter).  The game seems to have two big drawbacks:  1) the arbitrary limitations and 2) the damn action assignments.

1) The game has two major restraints that are completely arbitrary.  It's not like arbitrary limitations on things is anything new to Euros, but, since this game is so much like a warish-type game, they become glaringly obvious.  The first is the 10 card limit on actions.  You can have any number of provinces, so why is there a limit of 10?  Secondly, the limit of 18 rice.  Why can I only produce 18 rice?  That's totally stupid. I should be able to produce as much rice as my provinces are able.

2) I don't know about you, but sitting around and trying to assign my action cards is egregiously aggravating for me.  It's not like it's just some piece of extraneous chrome that only rears its ugly head once in a while; it's an integral part of the game.  It's annoying, disrupts the pace of the game, takes forever and makes me want to cut myself.
